Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Sale of a Runaway Slave and Effect of Return
Ruling on sale of a runaway slave and effect of return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a runaway slave, explain why return does not validate the contract, and distinguish the relied-upon position from the weak narration.

Core Idea
A void contract cannot be revived by later events; a new contract is needed.

This lesson deals with a classic fiqh scenario: selling a runaway slave who later returns. The key principle is that the object of sale must be capable of delivery at the time of contract. If it is not, the contract is void ab initio, and even if the impediment is later removed, the contract does not become valid. We will examine the relied-upon Hanafi position and a minority view from Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of a runaway slave (ābiq kāmil) where the slave cannot be delivered by the seller at the time of contract. The ruling: the sale is void (bāṭil).
A void contract (bāṭil) has no legal effect from the outset.
2
Locate the dalīl
The text provides two proofs: (1) the sale of a complete runaway is impermissible by consensus, and (2) the seller cannot deliver the object, so it is a sale of something not capable of delivery. The second is supported by qiyās to selling a bird in the air.
The analogy (qiyās) uses the same 'illah (effective cause): inability to deliver at the time of contract.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The lack of ability to deliver (qudrah ʿalā al-taslīm) is a condition for a valid sale. Since it is missing, the contract is bāṭil. Even if the slave returns later, the original contract cannot be validated because the condition was absent at the time of contract.
This is a strict application of the condition; it must be present at the moment of contracting.
4
Note the ikhtilāf
A narration from Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad holds that if the contract was not rescinded before the slave returned, the sale becomes valid upon return. Their reasoning: the slave's value remains, so the contract was valid but suspended by the impediment of non-delivery; when the impediment is removed, the contract is completed.
This view treats the contract as mawqūf (suspended), not bāṭil.
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ
The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is the Zāhir al-Riwāyah view: the contract is bāṭil and cannot be validated. The minority narration is not adopted because a void contract differs from a suspended one; the absence of a condition of validity cannot be cured later.
The text also notes that if the qāḍī rescinded the contract before the slave returned, then even according to the minority view the sale is not revived.

جدید کی ضرورت پڑے گی۔
And if the buyer said, 'The slave is with so-and-so, sell him to me,' and the master sold him to him, then this sale is also not permissible, because in this case the slave is a runaway with respect to both the seller and the buyer. So this is a complete runaway (abiq kamil), and the sale of a complete runaway is not permissible. Therefore its sale is impermissible. The second proof is that the seller is not able to hand him over, and the sale of something not capable of being delivered is impermissible, so its sale is also impermissible. And if someone sold a runaway slave and then the slave returned, the mentioned contract will not be completed; rather, a new contract will be required, because the contract for a runaway slave is void. It is void because the sold commodity must be capable of delivery at the time of sale, and at the time of sale the ability to deliver was lacking, so it was as if the object of sale was completely non-existent. When the object of sale is non-existent, the sale is void. Therefore, in this case the sale was void, and a void thing can never become valid. So the mentioned sale does not become valid by the slave's return; rather, a new sale is required, just as if someone sold a bird in the air and then the seller caught it and handed it over to the buyer in the contract session, the sale would not be valid. A narration from Imam Abu Hanifa is that if the contract was not rescinded before the slave returned, then the mentioned contract will be completed, because the value of the runaway slave remains, so the contract was concluded. This is why if the master frees or makes a mudabbar of a runaway slave, it is valid. If the slave's value had been eliminated due to running away, how could the master's freeing or making mudabbar be valid? Yes, the impediment to the validity of the sale was the seller's inability to hand over the slave, but when the slave returned, this impediment ceased. And when the impediment ceased, there is no problem in the sale being completed. And this is like when a slave runs away after the contract but before the buyer takes possession, the sale is completed. This is also narrated from Imam Muhammad. And if someone sold a runaway slave, the buyer filed a case in the qadi's court and demanded the slave from the seller, but the seller was unable, so the qadi rescinded the contract. Then after that, the runaway slave returned. In that case, the mentioned sale will not be completed; rather, a new sale will be required.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on al-Hidayah, addresses the ruling on selling a runaway slave and whether its subsequent return validates the original contract. The author first presents the case: if a buyer purchases a slave that is a runaway from both the seller and himself (i.e., the buyer also cannot control it), then the sale is invalid. The reason is twofold: first, it is a sale of a 'complete runaway' (abiq kamil); second, the seller is unable to deliver the object. The general Hanafi principle is that a sale contract requires the object to be capable of delivery (maqdur al-tasleem) at the time of contract. If the slave returns later, does the sale become valid? The text states the relied-upon position: no, because the contract was originally void (batil) due to the absence of a condition of validity, and a void contract cannot become valid later. A new contract is required. This is illustrated with the analogy of selling a bird in the air; even if later caught and delivered, the original sale is not valid. Then an intra-Hanafi narration from Imam Abu Hanifa is mentioned: if the contract was not rescinded before the slave returned, it becomes valid. This is based on the view that the slave's value remained intact, so the contract was valid but suspended by the impediment of non-delivery; when the impediment is removed, the contract becomes effective. The passage also mentions that Imam Muhammad agrees with this. Finally, a different scenario: if the buyer took the case to court and the qadi rescinded the contract before the slave returned, then the sale is definitely not revived. The lesson underscores the importance of the condition of delivery at the time of contract and the distinction between void (batil) and suspended (mawquf) contracts.

Key Terms
آبق کامل (ābiq kāmil): A slave who is a runaway with respect to both the seller and the buyer; neither can control him.
مقدور التسلیم (maqdūr al-taslīm): The object of sale must be capable of being delivered at the time of contract; a condition for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a runaway slave is sold and then returns without the contract having been rescinded, what is the Hanafi ruling according to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position?
A) The sale becomes valid automatically.
B) The sale remains void and a new contract is required.
C) The sale is valid only if the buyer did not know the slave was a runaway.
D) The sale becomes valid if the seller delivers the slave within three days.

Key Takeaway
A void contract cannot be cured by later events; the condition of delivery must exist at the time of contract.

Prohibition of Selling Human Milk: Hanafi Position and Proofs
Hanafi ruling on selling human milk with inter-madhhab dispute and intra-Hanafi difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling human milk, explain the two proofs (human parts not property and human dignity), and distinguish the Shafi'i position and the intra-Hanafi difference from Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Human milk is not property; selling it violates human dignity and is therefore invalid in the Hanafi school.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a debated issue: can a woman sell her milk? The Hanafi school firmly prohibits it, while the Shafi'i school permits it, considering it a pure drink like animal milk. The passage also records an intra-Hanafi narration from Abu Yusuf allowing the sale of a slave woman's milk, which is refuted by the majority. We will analyze the proofs and understand why human parts are not considered property in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions
Identify what each school rules regarding the sale of a woman's milk. The Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah) says it is invalid for both free and slave women. The Shafi'i school says it is valid because it is a pure drink.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'i opinion analogizes human milk to animal milk: both are pure drinks. Identify the illa (effective cause) they use: purity and drinkability.
Hanafis reject this analogy because the illa is incomplete; they add the condition of being property (mal).
3
Examine the Hanafi proofs
The passage gives two proofs: (1) human milk is a part of a human being, and human parts are not considered property (mal) since people do not trade them for wealth. (2) Human beings are honored (mukarram) and cannot be degraded by sale. Selling a human part would dishonor the whole.
These proofs rely on the concept that property is defined by customary trading (tamawwul).
4
Address the intra-Hanafi difference
A narration from Abu Yusuf (Nawadir) permits the sale of a slave woman's milk, reasoning that since the slave herself can be sold, her part can be sold too. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the self (dhat) and the part (juz'): slavery (riqq) attaches to the living self, not to the milk, which has no life. Therefore, milk has no property value.
This refutation uses the concept that riqq requires a living subject capable of freedom.
5
Determine the practical consequence and relied-upon position
The consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under Hanafi law, a woman cannot sell her milk; any such contract is void. Under Shafi'i law, it is valid. The mufta bihi is the Hanafi prohibition, and Abu Yusuf's view is not relied upon.
The text emphasizes that no difference exists in Zahir al-Riwayah between free and slave women.

اور جب مالیت ثابت نہیں تو باندی کے دودھ کی بیچ بھی درست نہ ہوگی اور باندی کی ذات میں چونکہ رقیت موجود ہے اس لئے اس میں مالیت بھی ثابت ہے اور جب مالیت ثابت ہے تو باندی کی بیع بھی جائز ہوگی۔ بہر حال جز یعنی دودھ اور گل یعنی باندی کی ذات کے درمیان اس قدر فرق کے ہوتے
He said: And there is no sale of a woman's milk in a cup. Al-Shafi'i said: Its sale is permissible because it is a pure drink. For us (Hanafi), it is a part of a human being, and a human being with all its parts is honored, protected from being degraded by sale. In the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), there is no difference between the milk of a free woman and a slave woman. From Abu Yusuf, it is narrated that the sale of a slave woman's milk is permissible, because it is permissible to enter a contract on her self, so likewise on her part. We say: the 'riqq' (slavery) has entered into her self; as for the milk, there is no riqq in it, because riqq is specific to a vessel in which the force that is its opposite (i.e., life) is realized, and that vessel is a living person, and there is no life in milk. Translation: He said that the sale of a woman's milk in a cup is also not permissible. Imam al-Shafi'i said that its sale is permissible because it is a pure drink. Our proof is that milk is a part of a human being, and a human being with all its parts is honored and protected from the degradation of sale. According to Zahir al-Riwayah, selling a free woman or a slave woman is permissible? No, the text says: 'and according to Zahir al-Riwayah, selling a free woman and a slave woman's self is not permissible?' Wait, the Urdu commentary says: 'اور ظاہر الروایہ کے مطابق آزاد عورت اور باندی کی ذات پر بیع وارد کرنا جائز ہے' meaning 'According to Zahir al-Riwayah, entering into a sale contract on the self of a free woman and a slave woman is permissible.' This seems contradictory because free persons cannot be sold. There might be an OCR corruption: 'آزاد عورت' should be 'ام ولد' or something? Actually, in the Urdu, it says 'آزاد عورت اور باندی کی ذات پر بیع وارد کرنا جائز ہے' which is incorrect for a free woman. This appears to be an error in the OCR or the original text. Possibly it means 'the sale of a slave woman is permissible' and the comparison is between a slave woman and her part. The Hanafi response then says: 'We will reply that riqq has pervaded her self, but as for the milk, there is no riqq in it.' So the original Arabic likely says: 'ولنا أن اللبن جزء الآدمي ... ولا فرق في ظاهر الرواية بين لبن الحرة والأمة' and then the commentary explains that there is no difference between free and slave regarding the prohibition. The Urdu seems garbled. We will note this in ocr_notes.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage deals with the issue of selling human milk, whether from a free woman or a slave woman. The Hanafi position (according to Zahir al-Riwayah) is that it is impermissible. The Shafi'i school permits it, considering it a pure drink (like animal milk). The passage provides two Hanafi proofs: First, human milk is a part of a human being, and human parts are not considered property (mal) in Islamic law because they are not traded for wealth (tamawwul). The second proof is that human beings are honored (mukarram) and cannot be subjected to the degradation of sale. The sale of a human part would imply dishonor to the whole. The passage then addresses an intra-Hanafi difference: a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf (from the Nawadir) allows the sale of a slave woman's milk, reasoning that since the slave woman herself can be sold, her part (milk) can also be sold. The Hanafi response refutes this by distinguishing between the self (dhat) and the part (juz'). Slavery (riqq) attaches to the self of the slave woman, but not to the milk, because riqq requires a living subject (where freedom is the opposite), and milk has no life. Therefore, milk possesses no property value (maliat) and cannot be sold, even if it comes from a slave woman. The free woman's milk is even more clearly prohibited.

Key Terms
جزء الآدمی (juz' al-ādami): A part of a human being, such as milk, hair, or limbs; considered not to be property (mal) in Hanafi law.
رقیت (riqqiyyah): The state of being a slave (slavery); it attaches to the living human being, not to separated parts.
مشروب طاهر (mashrūb tāhir): A pure (ritually clean) drink; the Shafi'i argument considers human milk as such, comparing it to animal milk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah), is the sale of a slave woman's milk valid?
A) Yes, because her self can be sold, so her parts can be sold too.
B) No, because milk has no life and thus no riqq, so it lacks property value.
C) Yes, if the milk is in a cup.
D) No, only if she is a free woman.

Key Takeaway
Human milk cannot be sold in the Hanafi school because it is a human part, not property, and selling it undermines human dignity.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Ruling on Selling Human Milk: Hanafi Prohibition and Proofs
Discusses prohibition of selling human milk with proofs and inter-madhhab responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling human milk, explain the proofs based on human dignity and absence of property value, and refute the Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf counterarguments.

Core Idea
Selling human milk is haram because a human part cannot be property and human dignity prohibits commodification.

The sale of human milk raises a fundamental question: can a part of a human being be treated as a commodity? In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling, which firmly prohibits the sale, and the proofs that distinguish human milk from animal milk. We also address the objections of Imam al-Shafi'i and a rare narration from Imam Abu Yusuf, clarifying why the Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Determine the hukm (legal ruling) on selling human milk according to the Hanafi school. Note the conditions: free or slave, milk in breast or vessel.
The ruling is haram (prohibited) in all cases, per Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
Examine the two proofs: (1) human milk is a part (juz') of a human, and a human is honored (mukarram) and cannot be subjected to sale; (2) milk lacks property value (maliyyah) because it is not customarily traded.
The key concept: a human part is not mal (property) in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Evaluate the Shafi'i counterproof and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that milk is a pure drink (sharab tahir), thus salable like cow's milk. The Hanafi response: not an absolute drink (haram after weaning); even if needed, necessity does not confer property value (like carrion).
The distinction between absolute and conditional drinkability.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi disagreement and its resolution
A narration from Imam Abu Yusuf (in Nawadir) permits sale of a slave girl's milk by analogy to the sale of her person. The response: riqqiyyah (servitude) requires life, but milk has no life, so the analogy fails. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) prohibits unconditionally.
Zahir al-Riwayah is the authoritative transmission.

Book of Sales: There is no difference [between milk in the breast and in a vessel] because it is specific to a substance that possesses the quality that is its opposite—life—and milk has no life. Translation: He said that the sale of a woman's milk that is in a cup is not permissible. Imam al-Shafi'i said its sale is permissible because it is a pure drink. Our proof is that milk is a part of a human being, and a human being is honored with all his parts and protected from the humiliation of sale. According to Zahir al-Riwayah, it is permissible to contract a sale on the person of a free woman and a slave girl; similarly, it should be permissible to contract on her part. We will reply: Servitude (riqqiyyah) has entered into her person, but as for milk, there is no servitude in it because servitude is attached to a place in which the quality that is its opposite—freedom—is realized, and that place is a living person, and there is no life in milk. Explanation: The issue is that the sale of a woman's milk is impermissible, whether the milk is in the breast or in a vessel, whether the woman is free or a slave. Imam al-Shafi'i said the sale of a woman's milk is permissible, whether the woman is free or a slave. There is a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf in Nawadir that the sale of a slave girl's milk is permissible, although the sale of a free woman's milk is impermissible. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is that a woman's milk is a pure drink, and a pure drink's sale is permissible, like the sale of cow's milk. By restricting 'drink' and 'pure', wine is excluded because its sale is impermissible due to its impurity. Our proof is that a woman's milk is a part of a human being, and a human being's part is not property; what is not property cannot be sold. So the sale of a woman's milk is impermissible. The second proof is that a human being is honored and protected from the indignity of sale; if milk were sold, it would imply the human part is not honored. Imam Abu Yusuf's proof: Since the sale of a slave girl's person is permissible, the sale of a part of her—her milk—should also be permissible by analogy. Our response: Servitude exists in her person but not in her milk because servitude is specific to a place where its opposite (freedom) can exist, and freedom exists only where there is life. Milk has no life, so it cannot be a place for servitude, hence no property value. Therefore, the analogy is incorrect. Response to Imam al-Shafi'i: 'Drink' either means absolutely or at the time of need. If absolutely, it is not accepted because after the weaning period, drinking a woman's milk is forbidden. If at the time of need, we accept that it is food for the child during the nursing period, but being food at the time of need does not make it property—just as carrion is food in necessity but not property.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on selling human milk and presents proofs from different scholars. The passage begins by stating the ruling: selling a woman's milk is impermissible, whether the milk is in the breast or in a container, and whether the woman is free or a slave. This is the Hanafi position. The commentator then presents the view of Imam al-Shafi'i, who permits the sale, arguing that human milk is a pure drink like cow's milk. The Hanafi response is given: human milk is a part of the human body, and a human being is honored (mukarram) and not subject to sale. Thus, selling a part of a human is disrespectful and not allowed. Furthermore, the Hanafis argue that milk is not property (mal) because people do not accumulate wealth through it; it does not have monetary value in transactions. An intra-Hanafi difference is mentioned from Nawadir: Imam Abu Yusuf is reported to have allowed the sale of a slave girl's milk, applying analogy from the sale of her person. The commentary refutes this by differentiating between the person (which has servitude, riqqiyyah) and milk (which has no life and thus no capacity for servitude). Since milk lacks life, it cannot be a locus for servitude, hence no property value attaches to it. Finally, a response to Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is given: human milk is not an absolute drink because after the nursing period it becomes forbidden; if it is considered a drink only at the time of need (like carrion), then being food in necessity does not make it property. Thus, the analogy with cow's milk fails.

Key Terms
جزء (juz'): A part of a human being, which is not considered property (mal) in Hanafi fiqh.
رقیت (riqqiyyah): Legal servitude or slavery. It attaches only to a living being capable of freedom.
مالیت (māliyyah): Property value or consideration in commercial transactions. Milk lacks this because it is not customarily traded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Selling human milk is impermissible in Hanafi fiqh because it is a human part, not property, and human dignity prohibits its commodification.

Ruling on Selling Pig Hair and Its Effect on Water
Explains prohibition of selling pig hair and disagreement on water impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling pig hair and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding its effect on a small quantity of water.

Core Idea
Pig hair is najis al-ayn: its sale is haram, use is permitted by necessity, and its effect on water is disputed between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses two interconnected questions: the permissibility of selling pig hair and the purity of water after a pig hair falls into it. While all Hanafi imams agree that sale is impermissible, they differ on whether the permission to use pig hair implies its purity for other contexts. This lesson will walk you through the legal reasoning and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Primary Ruling on Sale
Locate the explicit statement: 'لا يجوز بيع شعر الخنزير' (the sale of pig hair is not permissible). Recognize that the basis is najis al-ayn.
This is a clear prohibition (haram).
2
Understand the Exception: Benefit by Necessity
Note that benefiting from pig hair is allowed for sewing (khurz) out of necessity. The text says 'ويجوز الانتفاع به للخرز للصورة' (it is permissible to benefit from it for sewing).
Necessity makes the impermissible permissible, but only for use, not for sale.
3
Analyze the Rationale for Prohibition
Two reasons: (1) pig is najis al-ayn, so selling it is a humiliation; (2) pig hair is mubah al-asl (originally ownerless), so there is no need to buy/sell it. If it were not freely available, buying becomes permissible as per Faqih Abu al-Layth.
The distinction between necessity use and commercial transaction is key.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Water
Read the disagreement: Abu Yusuf holds that a pig hair falling into small water spoils it; Muhammad holds it does not. Each bases his view on the permission to benefit: Muhammad sees it as evidence of purity, Abu Yusuf sees it as limited to necessity.
The passage does not give a tarjih (preference).
5
Distinguish the Two Positions and Their Bases
Compare the dalil of each imam: Muhammad uses the general permission (itlaq al-intifa) to argue purity; Abu Yusuf restricts that permission to the state of use only, not to accidental fall.
The thamarah (practical consequence) is whether water can be used for purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الماء القليل افسد عند ابي يوسف وعند محمد لايفسده لان اطلاق الانتفاع به دلیل طهارته ولا بي يوسف ان الاطلاق للضرورة فلا تظهرا لا فى حالة الاستعمال وحالة الوقوع تغايرها ترجمہ اور سور کے بالوں کی بیع بھی نا جائز ہے کیونکہ سور نجس العین ہے لہذا اس کی اہانت کے واسطے اس کی بیع جائز نہیں ہے اور اس سے سینے کے ذریعہ ضرورت کی وجہ سے نفع اٹھانا جائز ہے اس لئے کہ یہ کام بغیر سور کے بالوں کے حاصل نہیں ہوتا اور کو خراب نہیں کریگا کیونکہ اس سے نفع اٹھانے کی اجازت اس کے پاک ہونے کی دلیل ہے اور ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ انتفاع کی اجازت ضرورت کی وجہ سے ہے پس حالت استعمال کے علاوہ میں یہ بات ظاہر نہ ہوگی اور پانی میں گرنے کی حالت اس سے مغامر ہے۔ تشریح تمام ائمہ کا اتفاق ہے کہ سور کے بالوں کی بیچ نا جائز ہے کیونکہ سوراپنی ذات ہی سے ناپاک اور نجس ہے اور نجس العین کی تو ہین و تذلیل کے پیٹی نظر اس کی بیع جائز نہیں ہوتی اس لئے سور کے بال کی بیع نا جائز ہے ہاں جوتے وغیرہ سینے کے لئے ضرورۃ سور کے بالوں سے نفع حاصل کرنا جائز ہے کیونکہ عادہ اس کام میں سور کے بال ہی استعمال کئے جاتے ہیں لیکن اگر سور کے بالوں کے علاوہ سے یہ کام چل سکتا ہو تو پھر سور کے بالوں کا استعمال کرنا درست نہ ہو گا۔ (فتح القدير ) چنانچہ بعض بزرگوں سے منقول ہے کہ وہ سور کے بال سے سلا ہوا موزہ نہیں پہنتے تھے اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ جوتا اور موزہ سور کے بال کے علاوہ سے بھی سلا جاتا تھا۔ ویوجد مباح الاصل سے سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ بقول صاحب ہدایہ کے جب ضرورۃ سور کے بالوں سے نفع اٹھانا جائز ہے تو اس کی بیچ بھی جائز ہونی چاہئے تھی۔ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ سور کے بال مباح الاصل ہیں یعنی مفت دستیاب ہو جاتے ہیں اس لئے ان کی خرید فروخت کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ لیکن اگر بغیر خرید کئے دستیاب نہ ہوتے ہوں تو ان کا خریدنا بھی جائز ہے جیسا کہ فقیہ ابواللیث نے فرمایا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ اگر سور کے بال ایسے پانی میں گر پڑے جو پانی نہ جاری ہو اور نہ وہ دردہ ہو یعنی ما تقلیل میں گر گئے تو
The sale of pig hair: He said: The sale of pig hair is not permissible because the pig is impure by essence (najis al-ayn), so its sale is not permissible as a humiliation for it. However, it is permissible to benefit from it for sewing (khurz) out of necessity, because that work cannot be done without it. And since it exists as mubah al-asl (ownerless), there is no need for sale. If it falls into a small amount of water, according to Abu Yusuf it spoils the water, but according to Muhammad it does not spoil it, because the permission to benefit from it indicates its purity. According to Abu Yusuf, the permission is due to necessity, so it does not appear except in the state of use, and the state of falling is different. Explanation: All imams agree that the sale of pig hair is impermissible because the pig is inherently impure, so its sale is not allowed as a form of humiliation. However, benefiting from it for sewing shoes etc. is permissible out of necessity, because customarily pig hair is used for that purpose. But if the work can be done without pig hair, then using it is not correct. (Fath al-Qadir) It is narrated that some scholars did not wear socks sewn with pig hair, indicating that such socks could be sewn with other materials. The phrase 'wa yujad mubah al-asl' answers the question: If benefiting from pig hair is permissible out of necessity, why isn't its sale allowed? The answer is that pig hair is owned by no one initially (mubah al-asl), i.e., available for free, so there is no need to buy and sell it. However, if it is not available without purchase, then buying it is permissible, as stated by Faqih Abu al-Layth. The author of al-Hidayah said: If pig hair falls into water that is not flowing and not large (i.e., less than a well) then...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on selling pig hair and its effect on a small quantity of water. The initial ruling is clear: selling pig hair is impermissible because the pig is considered najis al-ayn (impure by essence). The sale of such an item is prohibited out of respect and to avoid commodifying impurity. However, an exception is made for using pig hair in sewing, particularly for shoes and similar items, because this is a customary necessity—the work often cannot be done without it. The permissibility of use is limited to necessity; if an alternative is available, using pig hair is not allowed. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if use is allowed, why is sale prohibited? The answer is that pig hair is mubah al-asl (originally ownerless and freely available), so there is no need to transact it. However, if it is not freely available, buying it becomes permissible, as noted by Faqih Abu al-Layth. Finally, the passage discusses an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding the effect of a pig hair falling into a small quantity of water (qalil). Abu Yusuf holds that the water becomes impure (najis), while Muhammad holds that it remains pure because the general permission to use pig hair indicates its purity. The text explains Abu Yusuf's position: the permission to use is only out of necessity, so it does not extend to other contexts like falling into water. The passage is cut off at the end, but the remaining part would likely discuss the conditions of water quantity.

Key Terms
نجس العین (najis al-ayn): Something that is inherently impure by its substance, like a pig.
مباح الاصل (mubah al-asl): Something that is originally ownerless and free to take (like wild plants).
ما تقلیل (ma' qalīl): A small quantity of water (less than the size of a well, i.e., less than 200 dirhams or about 10×10 cubits).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, if a pig hair falls into a small amount of water, what is the ruling?
A) The water remains pure
B) The water becomes impure
C) It is recommended to discard the water
D) Only the hair must be removed, water remains pure

Key Takeaway
The sale of pig hair is categorically prohibited, but its use is allowed only for necessity; the purity of water after contact is disputed among Hanafi imams.

Sale of Pig Hair and Its Effect on Water
Ruling on selling pig hair and intra-Hanafi dispute on water impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of pig hair, explain the rationale of najis al-ayn, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding water impurity.

Core Idea
Pig hair is najis al-ayn: sale is haram, use allowed under necessity, water impurity disputed within the school.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding pig hair. While all Hanafi imams agree that selling pig hair is prohibited due to its inherent impurity, they differ on whether a pig hair falling into a small amount of water renders it impure. The passage grounds the ruling in the principles of najis al-ayn and necessity (darurah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage rules on: sale of pig hair and use of pig hair for sewing. State the hukm level for each.
Sale: haram; Use: mubah under necessity.
2
Analyze the rationale for sale prohibition
The text states pig hair is najis al-ayn (impure in essence). Sale would be a degradation (ihanah). Understand why this reasoning leads to prohibition.
Najis al-ayn items cannot be sold as they are contemptible.
3
Understand the exception for use
Use is permitted out of necessity because sewing shoes typically requires pig hair. Note the condition: necessity (darurah) and that the item is mubah al-asl (freely available).
If freely available, sale is unnecessary. If not, some scholars permit purchase.
4
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute on water impurity
Two positions: Abu Yusuf says a pig hair spoils a small quantity of water; Muhammad says it does not. Trace each imam's dalil.
Muhammad uses permissibility of use as evidence of purity; Abu Yusuf restricts that permissibility to the specific need.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Abu Yusuf, wudu with that water is invalid; according to Muhammad, it is valid. No tarjih is given in this passage.
This is an unresolved ikhtilaf within the school.

قال إلا يجوز بيع شعر الخنزير لانه نجس العين فلا يجوز بيعه اهانة له ويجوز الانتفاع به للخرز للصورة فان ذالك العمل لا يتاتى بدونه ويوجد مباح الاصل فلا ضرورة الى البيع ولو وقع في الماء القيل افسد عند ابي يوسف وعند محمد لايفسده لان اطلاق الانتفاع به دلیل طهارته ولا بي يوسف ان الاطلاق للضرورة فلا تظهرا لا فى حالة الاستعمال وحالة الوقوع تغايرها

ترجمہ اور سور کے بالوں کی بیع بھی نا جائز ہے کیونکہ سور نجس العین ہے لہذا اس کی اہانت کے واسطے اس کی بیع جائز نہیں ہے اور اس سے سینے کے ذریعہ ضرورت کی وجہ سے نفع اٹھانا جائز ہے اس لئے کہ یہ کام بغیر سور کے بالوں کے حاصل نہیں ہوتا اور ( چونکہ ) یہ بال غیر مملوک طور پر پل جاتے ہیں اس لئے ان کو فروخت کرنے کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے اور اگر تھوڑے پانی میں سور کا بال گر پڑا تو ابو یوسف کے نزدیک پانی کو خراب کر دیگا اور امام محمد کے نزدیک اس کو خراب نہیں کریگا کیونکہ اس سے نفع اٹھانے کی اجازت اس کے پاک ہونے کی دلیل ہے اور ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ انتفاع کی اجازت ضرورت کی وجہ سے ہے پس حالت استعمال کے علاوہ میں یہ بات ظاہر نہ ہوگی اور پانی میں گرنے کی حالت اس سے مغامر ہے۔

تشریح تمام ائمہ کا اتفاق ہے کہ سور کے بالوں کی بیچ نا جائز ہے کیونکہ سوراپنی ذات ہی سے ناپاک اور نجس ہے اور نجس العین کی تو ہین و تذلیل کے پیٹی نظر اس کی بیع جائز نہیں ہوتی اس لئے سور کے بال کی بیع نا جائز ہے ہاں جوتے وغیرہ سینے کے لئے ضرورۃ سور کے بالوں سے نفع حاصل کرنا جائز ہے کیونکہ عادہ اس کام میں سور کے بال ہی استعمال کئے جاتے ہیں لیکن اگر سور کے بالوں کے علاوہ سے یہ کام چل سکتا ہو تو پھر سور کے بالوں کا استعمال کرنا درست نہ ہو گا۔ (فتح القدير ) چنانچہ بعض بزرگوں سے منقول ہے کہ وہ سور کے بال سے سلا ہوا موزہ نہیں پہنتے تھے اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ جوتا اور موزہ سور کے بال کے علاوہ سے بھی سلا جاتا تھا۔ ویوجد مباح الاصل سے سوال کا جواب ہے۔

سوال یہ ہے کہ بقول صاحب ہدایہ کے جب ضرورۃ سور کے بالوں سے نفع اٹھانا جائز ہے تو اس کی بیچ بھی جائز ہونی چاہئے تھی۔ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ سور کے بال مباح الاصل ہیں یعنی مفت دستیاب ہو جاتے ہیں اس لئے ان کی خرید فروخت کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ لیکن اگر بغیر خرید کئے دستیاب نہ ہوتے ہوں تو ان کا خریدنا بھی جائز ہے جیسا کہ فقیہ ابواللیث نے فرمایا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ اگر سور کے بال ایسے پانی میں گر پڑے جو پانی نہ جاری ہو اور نہ وہ دردہ ہو یعنی ما تقلیل میں گر گئے تو

حضرت امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک پانی ناپاک ہو جائیگا اور امام محمد کے نزدیک نا پاک نہ ہو گا ۔ امام محمد کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ سور کے بالوں سے نفع حاصل کرنے کی اجازت دینا ان کے پاک ہونے کی دلیل ہے اور پاک چیز کے گرنے سے پانی ناپاک نہیں ہوتا اس لئے سور کے بال گرنے سے پانی ناپاک نہ ہوگا۔ امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ سور کے بالوں سے نفع حاصل کرنیکی اجازت ضرورت کے پیش نظر ثابت ہے اور جو چیز ضرورۃ ثابت ہو اس کا حکم دوسرے کی طرف متعدی نہیں ہوتا اس لئے سور کے بالوں کی ضرورت سینے کے کام میں تو ظا ہوگی مگر اس کے علاوہ میں ظاہر نہ ہوگی اور پانی میں گرنے کی حالت چونکہ استعمال کی حالت کے مغایر ہے اس لئے پانی میں گرنے کی حالت میں ان کی ضرورت اور طہارت ظاہر نہ ہوگی اور جب اس حالت میں ان کی طہارت ظاہر نہ ہوئی تو ان کے پانی میں گرنے سے پانی نا پاک ہو جائے گا۔
He said: The sale of pig hair is not permissible because the pig is najis al-ayn (impure in itself), so its sale is not permissible as degradation of it. However, it is permissible to benefit from it for sewing shoes out of necessity, because that work cannot be accomplished without it, and it is found as mubah al-asl (unowned), so there is no need for sale. And if it falls into a small quantity of water, according to Abu Yusuf it spoils it, but according to Muhammad it does not spoil it, because the permissibility of benefiting from it is evidence of its purity. According to Abu Yusuf, the permissibility is due to necessity, so it does not appear except in the state of use, and the state of falling is different from it.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on pig hair (shar' al-khinzir). It begins with a clear ruling: sale is not permissible because the pig is najis al-ayn (impure in its essence). The reason given is that sale would be a form of degradation (ihanah) of something that is already contemptible. However, using pig hair for sewing footwear (khirz) is allowed out of necessity because that work typically requires pig hair. The text notes that pig hair is mubah al-asl (originally unowned), meaning it can be obtained freely, so there is no need to buy it. This answers a potential question: if use is allowed, why not sale? The answer is that sale is unnecessary because the hair is available without purchase. However, if it is not freely available, some scholars like Faqih Abu al-Layth permit buying it. The second part discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding whether a pig hair falling into a small amount of water (ma' qalil) makes it impure. Muhammad argues that the permissibility of use indicates purity, so the water remains pure. Abu Yusuf counters that the permissibility is only due to necessity and does not extend beyond the specific situation of sewing; contaminating water is a different context, so the hair should be considered impure in general. This disagreement is within the Hanafi school, both positions being from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Something that is impure in its very essence, not just due to an external factor.
مباح الاصل (mubah al-asl): Something that is originally permissible/unowned, not owned by anyone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Abu Yusuf thinks pig hair is najis al-ayn, while Muhammad thinks it is pure.
B) Abu Yusuf limits the permissibility of use to necessity, so it does not indicate general purity; Muhammad extends it.
C) Muhammad relies on a hadith, Abu Yusuf on qiyas.
D) The passage does not mention their reasons.

Key Takeaway
Pig hair is najis al-ayn, making its sale haram; use is allowed only under necessity, and its effect on water is disputed between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad without a preferred view.

Reconciling Sale as Honor vs Degradation in Hanafi Legal Reasoning
Resolving contradictory rationales for prohibition of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the same act of sale can be considered a cause of honor (i'zaz) for some objects and degradation (ihanah) for others, and apply this principle to new cases.

Core Idea
Sale confers value: it honors the contemptible and degrades the honorable.

The Hidayah seems to contradict itself: it claims that sale degrades a human (prohibiting sale of human milk) but also that sale would honor a pig (prohibiting sale of pig hair). This lesson shows that both rulings are consistent under the principle that sale gives monetary value. For a noble object like a human, receiving a price lowers its dignity; for a contemptible object like a pig, receiving a price elevates its status. The same action thus has opposite effects depending on the object's inherent worth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory statements
Locate the two passages from Hidayah: one saying sale is a cause of degradation (for human milk), the other saying sale is a cause of honor (for pig hair). Note the apparent conflict.
The contradiction is in the taghya (effective cause) of the prohibition.
2
Understand the underlying principle
Recognize that sale is an act that assigns monetary value to an object. This valuation can either raise or lower the object's status depending on its original nobility or contemptibility.
The principle is that value-conferring acts have relative effects.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each case
For the human: being extremely honored, being sold equates him with commodities, which is degrading. For the pig: being extremely contemptible, being sold grants it value, which is honoring. Thus sale is prohibited in both cases but for opposite reasons.
The end result is same (prohibition), but the rationale differs.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
Practice by reasoning about other objects: e.g., selling a sacred text (degrading), selling garbage (honoring). Determine whether sale would be prohibited and why.
Use the same i'zaz/ihanah framework.

Source Text

فوائد صاحب ہدایہ کی دو عبارتوں میں بظاہر تعارض معلوم ہوتا ہے کیونکہ گذشتہ مسئلہ میں عورت کے دودھ میں بیع کو دلیل اہانت قرار دیا ہے۔ چنانچہ فرمایا ہے کہ آدمی اپنے تمام اجزاء کے ساتھ مکرم ہوتا ہے اور بیع کی وجہ سے اس کا غیر مکرم اور بے وقار ہونا لازم آتا ہے اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ بیچ دلیل اہانت ہے۔ اور سور کے بالوں میں بیچ کو دلیل اعزاز قرار دیا ہے چنانچہ فرمایا ہے کہ سور کے بالوں کی بیچ اس لئے نا جائز ہے کہ ان کی اہانت کرنا مقصود ہے اور اہانت ہوتی ہے عدم جواز بیع سے اس لئے سور کے بالوں کی بیع کو نا جائز قرار دیا گیا ہے۔ اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ بیع دلیل اعزاز ہے یعنی اگر سور کے بالوں کی بیع جائز قرار دی گئی تو سور کے بال معزز اور مکرم ہو جائیں گے پس ثابت ہوا کہ بیع دلیل اعزاز ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ صاحب ہدایہ نے ایک جگہ بیع کو دلیل اعزاز قرار دیا اور ایک جگہ دلیل اہانت قرار دیا پس شئی واحد دلیل اعزاز اور دلیل اہانت کس طرح ہو سکتی ہے۔

جواب فعل واحد ایک شئی کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے اہانت اور دوسری شئی کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے اعزاز و اکرام ہو سکتا ہے مثلاً بادشاہ نے جاروب کش سے کہا کہ تم بھی لوگوں کے ساتھ دربار میں بیٹھ جاؤ تو بادشاہ کا یہ کہنا اس جاروب کش کیلئے انتہائی اکرام و اعزاز ہے اور اگر قاضی سے کہا کہ تم لوگوں کے ساتھ دبا میں بیٹھ جاو و بارہا کایہ کہنا قاضی کی اہانت ہے۔ ملاحظہ فرمائیے ایک فعل ایک کے لئے اعزاز اور دوسرے کیلئے اہانت ہے۔ اور مثلاً جلانا آدمی کے حق میں اہانت اور لکڑیوں کے حق میں اعزاز ہے۔ پس اسی طرح آدمی چونکہ انتہائی مکرم اور معزز ہے اس لئے اس کو یا اس کے کسی جز کو بیچنا اور اس کا عوض لینا اور اس کو جمادات کے ساتھ لاحق کر دینا اس کی تذلیل اور توہین ہے اس لئے عورت کے دودھ میں بیع کو دلیل اہانت قرار دیا گیا اور سور چونکہ انتہائی نجس اور قابل اہانت ہے اس لئے اگر اس کو مبیغ بنا کر اس کے عوض بدل لیا گیا تو سور معزز اور مکرم ہو جائے گا اس لئے سور اور اس کے بالوں کے حق میں بیع کو دلیل اعزاز قرار دیا گیا۔ (فتح القدير ) جمیل عفی عنہ
Benefits: There appears to be an apparent contradiction between two statements of the author of Hidayah. In the previous issue regarding woman's milk, sale was considered a means of degradation. He said that a human being is honorable in all his parts, and sale necessitates him being dishonored and degraded. Thus, sale is a cause of degradation. Regarding pig hair, sale was considered a means of honor. He said that the sale of pig hair is impermissible because their degradation is intended, and degradation occurs through the impermissibility of sale. So the sale of pig hair was declared impermissible. From this it is understood that sale is a cause of honor: if the sale of pig hair were permissible, then pig hair would become honorable and dignified. So the conclusion is that the author of Hidayah considered sale as a cause of honor in one place and as a cause of degradation in another. How can one thing be both a cause of honor and degradation? Answer: A single act can be degradation relative to one thing and honor relative to another. For example, if a king says to a sweeper, 'You too sit in the court with the people,' this is extreme honor for the sweeper. If he says to a judge, 'You sit in the court with the people,' that is degradation for the judge. See how the same act is honor for one and degradation for another. Another example: burning is degradation for a human but honor for firewood. Similarly, because a human is supremely honorable, selling him or any part of him, taking compensation, and equating him with inanimate objects is his degradation. Therefore, in the case of woman's milk, sale was considered a cause of degradation. And because the pig is extremely impure and worthy of degradation, if it were made an object of sale and compensation taken for it, the pig would become honorable. Therefore, regarding the pig and its hair, sale was considered a cause of honor. (Fath al-Qadir) Jamil Afa Anhu.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential confusion in the reasoning of the author of Hidayah. In the previous discussion on selling human milk, the author argued that sale is a form of degradation (ihanah) because a human being is honored and selling his body parts reduces his dignity. However, in the discussion on pig hair, the author argued that sale is prohibited because it would be a form of honor (i'zaz) for something that is inherently contemptible. The apparent contradiction is that sale cannot be both a cause of degradation and honor. The resolution provided is that the same action can have different effects depending on the object. For a noble object (like a human), sale is degrading because it lowers the noble to the level of commodities. For a contemptible object (like a pig), sale is honoring because it elevates something contemptible by giving it monetary value. This is illustrated with examples: a king inviting a sweeper to sit in his court is honor for the sweeper, but the same invitation to a judge would be degradation. Similarly, burning is degradation for a human but honor for firewood. Thus, the rulings are consistent: sale of human body parts is prohibited because it degrades the human, and sale of pig hair is prohibited because it would honor the pig. The principle is that sale confers value, and that value is inappropriate for both the extremely honored (human) and the extremely dishonored (pig).

Key Terms
اعزاز (i'zaz): Honor, elevation in status; in this context, the effect of sale on a contemptible object, giving it value and thus honor.
اہانت (ihanah): Degradation, humiliation; in this context, the effect of sale on a noble object like a human, lowering his status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the sale of pig hair prohibited based on the principle of 'i'zaz' (honor)?
A) Because pig hair is impure and selling it would be degrading.
B) Because selling pig hair would give it value and thereby honor something that is inherently contemptible.
C) Because pig hair has no benefit.
D) Because pig hair is owned by the state.

Key Takeaway
The same act of sale can be considered degrading for a noble object and honoring for a contemptible object, so the Hidayah's rulings are consistent.

Prohibition of Selling and Using Human Hair
Ruling on the sale and use of human hair with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling and using human hair, cite the dalil from the hadith, and explain the underlying principle of human dignity (mukram).

Core Idea
Human hair cannot be sold or used because the human body is honored, not degraded.

This lesson examines a clear Hanafi ruling that the sale and use of human hair is prohibited. The reasoning is rooted in the dignity of the human being (mukram) and is supported by a Prophetic hadith. Understanding this ruling clarifies the boundary between permissible use of animal products and the prohibition of commodifying human body parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the ruling and identify the principle
The passage first states the prohibition and then gives the reason: the human being is mukram (honored) and not mubtadhal (degraded). Recognize that this principle is the foundation for the ruling.
2
Locate the hadith dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'Allah curses the woman who adds hair and the woman who asks for it to be added.' Identify that this is a sahih hadith reported in Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the hadith proves the ruling)
The curse (la'n) indicates a major sin, thus a clear prohibition. The hadith directly involves using human hair for extensions, which falls under 'benefiting from human hair'.
4
Distinguish from animal hair
The last sentence says permission is only for animal fur/wool. Human hair is explicitly excluded. Contrast the allowed use of animal products with the prohibited use of human body parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انسان کے بالوں کی بیع اور ان سے انتفاع کا حکم

ولايجوز بيع شعور الانسان ولا الانتفاع به لان الادمى مكرم لا مبتذل فلا يجوزان يكون شئى من اجزائه مهانا مبتذلا وقد قال عليه السلام لعن الله الواصلة المستوصلة الحديث وانما يرخص فيما يتخذ من الوبر
Ruling on the sale of human hair and benefiting from it: It is not permissible to sell human hair nor to benefit from it, because the human being is honored and not degraded, so it is not permissible for any of his parts to be degraded and dishonored. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Allah curses the woman who adds hair and the woman who asks for it to be added' - the hadith. And permission is given only for what is taken from fur/wool.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This short passage states the Hanafi ruling on human hair. It is not permissible to sell human hair or to use it (e.g., for wigs or other purposes). The reasoning is that a human being is mukram (honored), not mubtadhal (degraded), so none of his body parts should be treated as degraded commodities. The hadith cited is the well-known narration: 'Allah curses the woman who adds hair (al-wasilah) and the woman who asks for it to be added (al-mustawsilah),' which refers to the practice of attaching hair extensions. This hadith is used as evidence for the impermissibility of using human hair. The last sentence states that permission is only given for what is taken from animal fur or wool (wabar), implying that human hair is excluded from that permission. The ruling is that selling and using human hair is haram. This is based on the principle of honoring the human being.

Key Terms
مکرم (mukram): Honored; the inherent dignity of a human being by virtue of being human.
مبتذل (mubtadhal): Degraded; the opposite of mukram. The human should not be treated as a degraded object.
واصلة (wasilah): A woman who adds hair (by attaching extensions) to her own or another's hair.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because human hair is impure.
B) Because a human being is honored, and selling his body parts would degrade him.
C) Because the Prophet specifically sold human hair.
D) Because human hair has no useful purpose.

Key Takeaway
The sale and use of human hair is haram because the human body is honored and cannot be commodified, as supported by the hadith cursing those who add hair.

Resolution of Apparent Contradiction on Sale Being Honor or Dishonor
Explains how one act can be honor and dishonor depending on subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the same act (sale) can be a cause of honor or dishonor depending on the subject, and apply this principle to distinguish rulings on prohibited sales.

Core Idea
Sale honors base things but dishonors noble ones; context determines its moral effect.

Al-Hidayah contains two rulings that seem contradictory: one says sale dishonors human milk, another says sale would honor pig bristles. How can sale be both? This passage resolves the contradiction by showing that the effect of sale depends on the status of the item sold—noble items are degraded, base items are elevated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Locate the two statements from al-Hidayah: one stating sale of woman's milk is a cause of dishonor (ihānah), the other stating sale of pig bristles is a cause of honor (i‘zāz). Understand why this seems contradictory: the same act cannot be both honor and dishonor universally.
The contradiction is only apparent because the subject differs.
2
Recognize the principle of relativity
The commentator argues that one act can be honor relative to one thing and dishonor relative to another. Examples from the passage: a king's command to sit can honor a sweeper but dishonor a judge; burning honors wood but dishonors a person.
The key is the inherent status (sharaf or danā‘ah) of the subject.
3
Apply the principle to the Hanafi rulings
Man is noble (mukarram), so selling any part of him degrades him → sale is a cause of dishonor for human milk. The pig is base (najis, worthy of dishonor), so selling it would elevate its status → sale is a cause of honor for pig bristles. Thus both prohibitions are consistent: the first prevents degradation, the second prevents elevation.
This explains why the two rulings have opposite rationales but lead to the same prohibition.
4
State the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that the rationale for prohibiting sale of human body parts is based on human dignity, while the rationale for prohibiting sale of impure objects is based on avoiding their honorification. This distinction guides fatwa in new cases.
For example, selling contaminated soil would be prohibited to prevent its elevation, while selling a sacred text would be prohibited to prevent its degradation.

Benefits: There appears to be an apparent contradiction between two statements of the author of al-Hidayah. In the previous issue, sale of a woman's milk was considered a cause of dishonor. Thus he said: 'Man is honored in all his parts, and by selling it becomes necessary for him to be dishonored and undignified.' From this it is understood that sale is a cause of dishonor. And in the issue of pig bristles, sale was considered a cause of honor. Thus he said: 'The sale of pig bristles is impermissible because dishonoring them is intended, and dishonor occurs by impermissibility of sale, therefore the sale of pig bristles is ruled impermissible.' From this it is understood that sale is a cause of honor, meaning: if the sale of pig bristles were permitted, they would become honored and dignified. So it is established that sale is a cause of honor. In summary, the author of al-Hidayah in one place considered sale a cause of honor and in another a cause of dishonor. So how can one thing be both a cause of honor and a cause of dishonor? Answer: One single act can be dishonor relative to one thing and honor relative to another. For example, the king told a sweeper: 'Also sit in the court with the people.' This statement of the king is extreme honor for the sweeper. If he told the judge: 'Sit with the people in the dust,' this statement is dishonor for the judge. Observe: one act is honor for one and dishonor for another. Similarly, burning is dishonor for a human and honor for wood. Thus, since man is extremely honored and dignified, selling him or any part of him, taking compensation, and equating him with inanimate objects is his degradation and humiliation. Therefore, in the case of woman's milk, sale was considered a cause of dishonor. And since a pig is extremely impure and worthy of dishonor, if it is made an object of sale and compensation is taken for it, the pig becomes honored and dignified. Therefore, in the case of a pig and its bristles, sale was considered a cause of honor. (Fath al-Qadir). Jameel 'Afī 'Anh.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (tahqīq) resolving an apparent contradiction between two rulings of al-Hidayah. In the previous lesson, the sale of human milk was prohibited because it degrades man. Here, the sale of pig bristles is also prohibited, but for the opposite reason: it would elevate the pig. The commentator, using a rational argument (talīl), explains that the same action (sale) can be either honor or dishonor depending on the subject. For a noble being (insān), sale is degradation because man is already honored. For a base thing (pig), sale is elevation because it gives value to something inherently worthless. The analogy with the king's command to a sweeper or judge illustrates that context determines the moral quality of an act. This resolution is taken from Fath al-Qadīr.

Key Terms
بیع (bay‘): Sale; the act of exchanging property for compensation.
اعزاز (i‘zāz): Honoring or elevating something's status.
اہانت (ihānah): Dishonoring or degrading something.

Key Takeaway

Selling Skins of Dead Animals: Before and After Tanning
States prohibition before tanning and permissibility after tanning with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the skin of a dead animal before and after tanning and explain the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Selling untanned skin (ihāb) of a dead animal is haram; after tanning (dibāgh) it becomes pure and permissible.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a clear ruling on the sale of animal skins from animals not slaughtered Islamically (mītah). The text distinguishes between the untanned skin (ihāb) and the tanned skin (adīm), highlighting that tanning transforms the object from impure to pure, thereby changing the legal status of its sale. We will examine the hadith proof and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its rulings
The act is selling the skin of a dead animal. Before tanning, the sale is prohibited (haram). After tanning, the sale is permissible (ja'iz).
The ruling changes based on the physical transformation.
2
Locate the dalil
The Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) said: 'Lā tantafi‘ū min al-maytati bi-ihāb' (Do not benefit from the skin of a dead animal).
This is an ahad hadith, zanni in transmission and meaning.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition of benefiting from the ihāb logically extends to selling, since selling is a form of benefit. Therefore, untanned skin cannot be sold.
The imperative 'lā tantafi‘ū' indicates prohibition (haram).
4
Understand the effect of tanning
Tanning (dibāgh) purifies the skin, making it pure (tāhir) and beneficial. Hence, after tanning, the sale becomes valid because the object is no longer impure.
This is based on the Hanafi principle that tanning purifies the skin of a dead animal (except dog and pig).
5
Note cross-madhhab consideration
The ruling is mukhtalaf fih; other schools may differ on the scope of purification (e.g., require the animal to be halal and slaughtered properly).
The Hanafi school is more permissive in this regard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا بيع جلود الميتة قبل ان تدبغ لا نه غير منتفع به قال عليه السلام لا تنتفعوا من الميتة باهاب وهو اسم لغير المدبوغ على ما مرفى كتاب الصلوة ولا باس ببيعها والانتفاع بها بعد الدباغ لانها طهرت بالدباغ وقد ذكرناه في كتاب الصلوة
He said: And there is no sale of the skins of dead animals before they are tanned, because there is no benefit from it. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not benefit from the skin (ihāb) of a dead animal' – and ihāb is the name for untanned skin – as mentioned in the Book of Prayer. And there is no harm in selling them and benefiting from them after tanning, because they become pure by tanning, and we have mentioned it in the Book of Prayer.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This is a clear ruling statement with proof. The text states that selling the skin of a dead animal (mītah – an animal not slaughtered Islamically) is prohibited before tanning, because it is impure and cannot be used for any permissible purpose. The proof is the Prophet's hadith: 'Do not benefit from the ihāb of a dead animal.' The term 'ihāb' refers specifically to untanned skin. After tanning, the skin becomes pure (tāhir) according to the Hanafi school, and thus it is permissible to sell it and use it. The commentator notes that this has been discussed earlier in the chapter on prayer (Kitāb al-Ṣalāh). The ruling is based on the purification by tanning (dibāgh).

Key Terms
اهاب (ihāb): The untanned skin of a dead animal.
دباغ (dibāgh): The chemical process of tanning that purifies the skin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when is it permissible to sell the skin of a dead animal?
A) At any time, if it is dried.
B) Only after tanning.
C) Only if it is from a halal animal.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
Selling the skin of a dead animal is prohibited before tanning due to impurity, but after tanning it becomes pure and permissible to sell.

Selling Human Hair: Prohibition and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with dalil naqli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on selling human hair, distinguish Imam Muhammad’s dissenting view, and explain the rationale of human dignity versus impurity.

Core Idea
Human hair cannot be sold because of human dignity, not impurity, despite the Prophet's own hair being a blessing.

This passage addresses a classic Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of human hair. The relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) prohibits it due to human dignity, while Imam Muhammad permits it based on the Prophet's distribution of his own hair for blessings. We will examine both proofs and the reasoning that settles the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being discussed (selling human hair) and the initial ruling given by the author (not permissible).
The ruling is explicitly stated at the beginning.
2
Locate the dalil for prohibition
Find the proof cited for the prohibition: the hadith cursing the wasilah and mustawsilah, and the principle of human dignity.
The dalil is both a hadith and a rational argument (karamah).
3
Understand the rationale: dignity vs impurity
The text clarifies that the prohibition is not due to impurity (najis) but because a human is honored (mukarram). Degrading any part of a human is impermissible.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the ikhtilaf.
4
Examine Imam Muhammad's view and his proof
Imam Muhammad permits benefiting from human hair. His proof: the Prophet distributed his shaved hair to Companions who sought blessings (tabarruk), indicating the hair is pure and usable.
Imam Muhammad uses the Prophet's act as evidence of permissibility.
5
Evaluate why the Zahir al-Riwayah position is mufta bihi
The author rebuts Imam Muhammad: the Prophet's distribution was for tabarruk, not for sale or general trade. The inkishaf (incident of drinking blood) shows impure things are not used for tabarruk, but the prohibition here is based on dignity, not impurity. The hadith of wasilah specifically condemns hair attachment, which is a form of degradation.
Thus, the mufta bihi is prohibition as in Zahir al-Riwayah.

جائز ہے۔
فوائد عورتوں کے بالوں کی جڑوں میں بال گوندے جاتے ہیں عورتوں کے بالوں کو گھنا اور زیادہ کرنے کے لئے اور اس کی زلفوں میں جوڑا جاتا ہے ان کو دراز اور لمبا کرنے کے لئے ۔
Heading: He adds to the locks of women their braids. Translation: Selling human hair and benefiting from it is not permissible, because a human being is honored, not degraded and dishonored. Therefore, it is not permissible to degrade and dishonor any part of him. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said that Allah curses the one who joins hair and the one who has it joined, and it is permitted to join hair taken from camels etc. to increase women's locks and braids. Explanation: The issue is that selling human hair and benefiting from it is not permissible. However, it should be noted that the impermissibility is not due to impurity, but due to human dignity. It is narrated from Imam Muhammad that benefiting from human hair is permissible. Imam Muhammad's proof is that when the Prophet (peace be upon him) shaved his head, he distributed his hair among the Companions, and they sought blessings from his hair. If the hair were impure and not usable, he would not have distributed them nor would the Companions have sought blessings, because blessings are not sought from impure things. As it is narrated that Abu Taybah drank the Prophet's blood intending blessings, but the Prophet forbade it. This shows that one does not seek blessings from impure things. Therefore, the Prophet's distribution of his hair and the Companions' seeking blessings from them indicate they are pure and usable. The reason for impermissibility in Zahir al-Riwayah is that a human being is honored, not degraded and dishonored. And something that is honored, it is not permissible to degrade any part of it. Selling hair and benefiting from it degrades human hair, so it is not permissible to sell human hair or benefit from it. This is supported by the Prophet's statement: 'Allah curses the wasilah and the mustawsilah.' Al-Wasilah is one who takes a woman's hair and attaches it to another woman's hair, and al-Mustawsilah is one who orders it. The Prophet cursed both. Thus it is established that benefiting from women's hair is not permissible, and if benefiting is not permissible, then selling is even more impermissible. However, hair from camels, goats etc. may be attached to women's hair. Benefits: Hair is attached to the roots of women's hair to make it thicker and fuller, and it is joined to their locks to lengthen and extend them.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of human hair. The author begins by stating the relied-upon (Zahir al-Riwayah) position: selling human hair is prohibited not because it is impure, but because of human dignity. A human is honored, and degrading any part of him is impermissible. The hadith cursing the wasilah (one who attaches hair) and mustawsilah (one who requests it) supports this. Then Imam Muhammad's divergent view is presented: he deems benefiting from human hair permissible, citing the Prophet's distribution of his own hair to the Companions, who sought blessings from it, indicating the hair is pure and usable. The author explains that Imam Muhammad's evidence is rebutted by the principle that blessings are not sought from impure things (as shown by the incident with Abu Taybah drinking the Prophet's blood, which the Prophet forbade). However, the Zahir al-Riwayah's reasoning is that the impermissibility stems from human dignity, not impurity. The passage concludes that while human hair cannot be sold, hair from animals like camels and goats may be attached to women's hair. The final 'Benefits' section clarifies the practice of hair attachment for thickening or lengthening women's hair.

Key Terms
واصلہ (wāṣilah): One who takes a woman's hair and attaches it to another woman's hair.
مستوصلہ (mustawṣilah): One who orders or requests the attachment of hair to her own hair.
تبرک (tabarruk): Seeking blessings (barakah) from a sacred object, such as the Prophet's hair.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah position, why is selling human hair prohibited?
A) Because human hair is impure (najis).
B) Because selling hair degrades the honor of a human being.
C) Because the Prophet prohibited all trade in hair.
D) Because human hair has no beneficial use.

Key Takeaway
Selling human hair is haram due to human dignity (karamah), not impurity, and this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sale of Hides, Bones, and Other Parts of Dead Animals; Status of Elephant
Rulings on dead animal parts and elephant dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling dead animal hides and other parts and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the elephant's legal status.

Core Idea
Dead animal hides require tanning to become sellable; bones and hair are inherently pure; the elephant is like a beast of prey, not najis al-‘ayn.

This lesson covers two related rulings: the sale of dead animal hides and other body parts, and the legal status of the elephant. We will first see why hide before tanning is prohibited, then why bones, sinews, wool, horn, and hair are permissible. Finally, we examine the intra-Hanafi difference on whether the elephant is najis al-‘ayn like a pig or like a beast of prey.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on dead animal hide before tanning
Recognize that selling the raw hide is impermissible because it is impure and not legally beneficial (ghayr muntafi‘ bih).
The hadith 'La tantafi‘u min al-maytati bi-ihab' is the key proof.
2
Understand the effect of tanning
Tanning (dibagh) purifies the hide, making it pure and beneficial, thus its sale becomes permissible.
Tanning removes the impure moisture; it is analogous to slaughter purifying the hide.
3
Learn the ruling on bones, sinews, wool, horn, and hair
These parts are inherently pure because they lack life (hayat); death does not affect them. Therefore, selling them and benefiting from them is permissible.
This is a general rule applied to all such parts from dead animals.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the elephant
Imam Muhammad considers the elephant najis al-‘ayn like a pig. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (al-Shaykhayn) consider it like a beast of prey; its essence is pure, only flesh and saliva are impure.
The practical effect: under Muhammad's view, nothing from the elephant can be sold; under Shaykhayn, bone and other parts are sellable.
5
Evaluate the evidence for the Shaykhayn's view
The hadith that the Prophet purchased ivory bangles for Fatima indicates that ivory (elephant bone) is pure and permissible to use.
This hadith outweighs the analogy to the pig.

اس سے نفع اٹھانے میں کچھ مضائقہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ دباغت سے وہ پاک ہو گئی ہے اور ہم اس کو کتاب الصلوۃ میں ذکر کر چکے ہیں ۔ تشریح مسئلہ مردار کی غیر مد بوغ کھال کی بیع نا جائز ہے کیونکہ دباغت سے پہلے مردار کی کھال نجس ہونے کی وجہ سے نا قابل انفاع ہے چنانچہ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا ارشاد ہے لا تنتفعو امن الميتة باهاب اور اهاب اس کی کھال کو کہتے ہیں جس کو دباغت نہ دی گئی ہو۔ اور جو چیز شرعا غیر منتفع بہ اور نجس ہو اس کی بیع جائز نہیں ہوتی ۔ اس لئے مردار کی کچی کھال کی بیچ ناجائز ہے ۔ ہاں دباغت کے بعد اس کا بیچنا بھی جائز ہے اور اس سے نفع اٹھانا بھی جائز ہے کیونکہ دباغت کی وجہ سے جب نجس رطوبات زائل ہو گئیں تو کھال پاک ہو جائے گی۔ جیسا کہ ذبح کرنے سے کھال پاک ہو جاتی ہے۔ اس مسئلہ کی پوری تفصیل کتاب الطہارت میں گذر چکی۔ مردار کی ہڈیوں، پٹھوں، اون، سینگ، بال، اونٹ کے بال کی بیج اور ان سے انتفاع کا حکم
ولا باس ببيع عظام الميتة وعصبها وصونها وقرنها وشعرها ووبرها والانتفاع بذالك كله لا نها طاهرة لا يحلها الموت لعدم الحيوة وقد قررناه من قبل والفيل كالخنزير نجس العين عند محمدوعند هما بمنزلة السباع حتى يباع عظمه وينتفع به

Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary volume 8. Translation: And the sale of a dead animal's hide before tanning is not permissible because it is not beneficial. The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: Do not benefit from the raw hide of a dead animal. 'Ihab' is the name for an untanned hide. As passed in the Book of Prayer. And after tanning, selling it and benefiting from it is not problematic because it is purified by tanning. And we have mentioned this in the Book of Prayer. Explanation of the issue: The sale of an untanned hide of a dead animal is impermissible because before tanning the hide is impure and thus not beneficial. Accordingly, the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: Do not benefit from the hide of a dead animal. And 'ihab' refers to its hide that has not been tanned. And something that is legally non-beneficial and impure, its sale is not permissible. Therefore the sale of a raw hide of a dead animal is impermissible. Yes, after tanning, selling it and benefiting from it is permissible because when the impure moisture is removed by tanning, the hide becomes pure. Just as the hide becomes pure through slaughter. The full detail of this issue has passed in the Book of Purification. Rulings on the sale of bones, sinews, wool, horn, hair, and camel hair of a dead animal and benefiting from them: And there is no harm in selling the bones of a dead animal, its sinews, its wool, its horn, its hair, and its fur, and benefiting from all that because they are pure; death does not affect them because they have no life. And we have established this before. And the elephant, according to Muhammad, is like the pig, being impure in essence; but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa), it is of the category of beasts of prey, so its bone can be sold and one may benefit from it. Translation: And there is no harm in selling the bones of a dead animal, its sinews, its wool, its horns, its hair, and the hair of a dead camel and benefiting from all that because these things are pure; death has not affected them because they had no life. And we have explained this previously. And according to Imam Muhammad, the elephant is like a pig, impure in essence. And according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), it is of the category of beasts of prey, such that its bone can be sold and benefit can be taken from it. Explanation: There is no harm in selling the bone of a dead animal, its sinews, the wool of a dead goat, its horn, the hair of other dead animals, and the hair of a dead camel, and benefiting from all that because these things are pure; they are pure because death does not affect them, and death does not affect them because they have no life. Its detail has been explained in the Book of Purification. Imam Muhammad said: The elephant is impure in essence like a pig; its hide will not be purified by slaughter nor by tanning. And the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) said: The elephant is in the category of beasts of prey; thus its saliva and flesh are impure, but its essence is not impure. Therefore, selling its bone etc. and benefiting from it is permissible. And riding the elephant itself and loading weight on it is permissible. And in a hadith: The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, purchased two bangles of elephant ivory for Fatima, may Allah be pleased with her. This hadith indicates that the elephant is not impure in essence like a pig.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya begins by addressing the sale of a dead animal’s hide. The ruling is that selling the hide before tanning is impermissible because it is both impure (najis) and not legally beneficial (ghayr muntafi‘ bih). The proof is the Prophet’s (صلى الله عليه وسلم) prohibition: “Do not benefit from the hide of a dead animal.” After tanning (dibagh), the hide becomes pure and can be sold and used. The author refers to the Book of Prayer for prior discussion. Next, the text expands to other parts of a dead animal: bones, sinews, wool, horn, hair, and camel hair. These are deemed pure and unaffected by death because they lack life (hayat). Hence, selling them and benefiting from them is permissible without any problem. This is the position of the Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah). The passage then addresses a point of intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the elephant (fil). Imam Muhammad considers the elephant to be najis al-‘ayn (impure in essence) like a pig, meaning its hide cannot be purified by slaughter or tanning. In contrast, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (al-Shaykhayn) categorize the elephant as a beast of prey (siba‘). Thus, its bone and other parts can be sold and used because its essence is not impure, while its flesh and saliva are impure. The author supports the Shaykhayn’s view with a hadith that the Prophet purchased ivory bangles for Fatima, showing that ivory is pure.

Key Terms
اهاب (ihab): The hide of an animal that has not been tanned.
نجس العين (najis al-‘ayn): Impure in essence; the substance itself is considered impure regardless of purification efforts.
دباغ (dibagh): Tanning; the process of treating hide to make it pure and usable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah), what is the ruling on selling the bones of a dead animal?
A) Permissible because they are pure
B) Impermissible because they are part of a dead animal
C) Permissible only after tanning
D) Impermissible unless the animal was slaughtered

Key Takeaway
The tanning of a dead animal's hide makes it sellable, while bones and hair are always pure; the elephant is akin to a beast of prey, not a pig, according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling Haqq al-Ta'li vs Sharb: Mal and the Subject of Sale
Ruling on selling haqq al-ta'li after collapse, with proof and comparison to sharb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish haqq al-ta'li from sharb in terms of being mal (property) and apply the Hanafi definition of mal to determine the validity of a sale.

Core Idea
A right attached to air is not property; water is property because it can be possessed.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents two related rulings: one concerning the sale of a right to rebuild an upper chamber (haqq al-ta'li) after a collapse, and another concerning the sale of an irrigation right (sharb). The key to both is the Hanafi definition of property (mal) as something that can be possessed. Understanding this distinction is crucial for assessing whether a given right can be the subject of a valid sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts and their rulings
First, identify that the passage rules on two separate sales: (1) selling haqq al-ta'li after the upper chamber collapses, and (2) selling sharb (as a follow to land or individually). Note that the first is ruled invalid, the second valid under certain conditions.
The first ruling is the core; the second is a comparative example.
2
Locate the dalil for the invalidity of selling haqq al-ta'li
The text states the reason: 'because the right of building is not property (mal), for property is what can be possessed.' This is the proof.
The dalil is a rational (aqli) one based on the legal definition of mal.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'possessed' (ihraz) is crucial. The right to build is attached to air, which cannot be physically possessed. Therefore, it is not mal, and the sale is invalid.
This distinguishes it from tangible property like water.
4
Analyze the comparison with sharb
Sharb is a share of water. Water is a tangible substance that can be possessed (ihraz). Hence, it is mal, and its sale is valid. The text notes an intra-Hanafi difference: selling sharb individually is allowed in one riwaya, preferred by the scholars of Balkh.
The comparison highlights that not all rights are equal; some are attached to tangible goods.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical difference is that selling sharb individually is valid according to a chosen view, while selling haqq al-ta'li individually is invalid. This affects transactions involving rights.
The mufta bihi for sharb is the permissive view.

بخلاف الشرب سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔
