سوال .... یہاں ایک سوال باقی رہ جاتا ہے وہ یہ کہ شرب کی بیع کو اس لئے جائز کہا گیا ہے کہ شرب پانی کے ایک حصہ کا نام ہے تو گویا بیچ پانی کی ہوئی اب اگر پانی کی گذرگاہ میں پانی موجود نہ ہو تو اس کی بیچ نا جائز ہونی چاہئے تھی کیونکہ معدوم کی بیع نا جائز ہوتی ہے حالانکہ اس کی بیع جائز ہے۔
جواب ضرورت کی وجہ سے پانی کے وجود کو فرض کر کے اس کی بیچ کو جائز قرار دے دیا گیا ہے جیسا کہ بیع سلم میں اور استصناع یعنی سائی دے کر کوئی چیز بنوانے میں مبیع معدوم ہوتی ہے لیکن اس کے باوجود بیع درست ہے پس اسی طرح اگر فی الوقت پانی موجود نہ ہو تو اس کا وجود فرض کر کے ضرورت کے پیش نظر شرب کی بیع کو جائز قرار دیا گیا ہے۔
He said: If the lower floor belongs to one person and its upper chamber to another, then both collapse or only the upper chamber collapses, and the owner of the upper chamber sells his upper chamber (right), it is not permissible because the right of building (haqq al-ta'li) is not property, for property is what can be possessed. Property is the subject of sale. Unlike irrigation right (sharb), where its sale is permissible as a follow to land by agreement of all narrations, and individually in one narration, and this is the choice of the scholars of Balkh, because it is a share of water, and for this reason it is guaranteed when destroyed, and it has a portion of the price as we mentioned in the Book of Irrigation. (Then Urdu commentary follows: explanation, a question about the comparison, and an answer: sharb is a share of water, water is actual property, so it is guaranteed, and it has a portion of the price. So sharb is property and its sale is valid, while haqq al-ta'li is not property. Then a question about selling sharb when water is absent: answer: out of necessity, water is considered existent, like in salam and istisna' contracts.)
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from al-Hidayah. It addresses the case where a lower floor and an upper chamber belong to different people, and both collapse or only the upper chamber collapses. If the owner of the upper chamber then sells his right to build (haqq al-ta'li), the sale is invalid because haqq al-ta'li is not considered property (mal). The text defines mal as something that can be possessed (ihraz). Since haqq al-ta'li pertains to air and space, it cannot be possessed, hence it is not mal. The text then compares this with sharb (right to irrigation water). Sharb is a share of water, which is mal because water can be possessed. Therefore, selling sharb as a follow to land is agreed upon, and selling it individually is allowed in one riwaya, which is the choice of the scholars of Balkh. The discussion continues with a question: if sharb is a share of water, what if the water is not currently present? The answer is that out of necessity, water is considered existent, similar to salam and istisna' contracts where nonexistent items are sold.

Key Terms
حق تعلی (haqq al-ta'li): The right to construct an upper chamber above someone else's property. In this passage, it is considered a right attached to air, not property.
شرب (sharb): The right to use water for irrigation, considered a share of water. It is treated as property (mal) because water can be possessed.
مال (mal): Property that can be possessed (ihraz) and has value. The text specifies that mal is the subject of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it is not considered property (mal) as it cannot be possessed.
C) Because the building has collapsed.
D) Because it is a right only for the owner of the lower floor.

Key Takeaway
A valid sale requires the subject matter to be mal (property); haqq al-ta'li is not mal because it is attached to air, while sharb is mal because it is attached to water.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Selling a Pathway vs. a Watercourse: Known vs. Unknown
Ruling on selling tariq and masil with two interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a pathway (tariq) and a watercourse (masil), distinguish between the two interpretations, and apply the reasoning based on known/unknown and permanence of the object.

Core Idea
A pathway is known and saleable; a watercourse is unknown and non-saleable — but the reason depends on whether the land or the right is sold.

This passage presents a nuanced ruling on selling a pathway and a watercourse. At first glance, it seems straightforward: pathways may be sold, watercourses may not. But the Hidayah author alerts us to two possible interpretations: are we selling the land itself or the right to use it? The answer affects the rationale. Understanding this distinction is key to mastering Hanafi sales law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
Read the first sentence: selling and gifting a pathway (tariq) is permissible; for a watercourse (masil) it is invalid. Note the ruling and the terms.
The ruling is stated without qualification.
2
Recognize the two possible interpretations
The text says the issue can be understood in two ways: (1) the object is the land itself (raqaba), or (2) the object is the right of passage (haqq al-murur) or right to channel water (haqq al-tasil). Identify these two layers.
This parsing is essential to correctly apply the ruling.
3
Analyze for the land itself (raqaba)
If the object is the land, the difference is that a pathway is known (ma'lum) because it has fixed dimensions, while a watercourse is unknown (majhul) because the amount of water it will carry is unpredictable. Thus the sale of the watercourse land is invalid due to uncertainty.
Known vs. unknown is a key concept in sales.
4
Analyze for the rights (haqq al-murur and haqq al-tasil)
If the object is the right of passage, there are two narrations. According to one, the right of passage is known because it attaches to a permanent known location (the pathway). The right to channel water: on a roof it resembles haqq al-ta'li (right to build), which is attached to a non-permanent building; on the ground it is unknown due to location uncertainty.
The comparison with haqq al-ta'li shows that rights attached to permanent entities are treated differently.
5
Note the difference between right of passage and haqq al-ta'li
The text explains that haqq al-ta'li attaches to a non-permanent entity (building), so it resembles usufruct (manafi') and is not saleable as a 'ayn. The right of passage attaches to permanent land, so it resembles tangible property (a'yan) and may be saleable.
Permanence of the underlying asset is the determining factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وبيع الطريق وهبته جائز وبيع مسيل الماء هبته باطل والمسالة تحتمل وجهين بيع رقبة الطريق والمسيل وبيع حق المرور والتسييل فان كان الاول فوجه الفرق بين المسالتين ان الطريق معلوم لان له طولا وعرضا معلوما واما المسيل فمجهول لانه لا يدرى قدر ما يشغله من الماء وان كان الثاني ففى بيع حق المرورروايتان ووجه الفرق على احدهما بينه وبين حق التسبيل أن حق المرور معلوم لتعلقه بمحل معلوم وهو الطريق اما المسيل على السطح فهو نظير حق التعلى وعلى الأرض مجهول لجهالة محله ووجه الفرق بين حق المرورو حق التعلى على احدى الروايتين ان حق تعلى يتعلق بعين لا تبقى وهو البناء فاشبه المنافع اما حق المروريتعلق بعين تبقى وهو الأرض فاشبه الاعيان
ترجمہ کہا اور راستہ کا بیچنا اور اس کا ہبہ کرنا جائز ہے اور پانی رواں ہونے کے راستہ کا بیچنا اور اسکا ہبہ کرنا باطل ہے۔ یہ مسئلہ دوصورتوں کا احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ تین طریں اور عین مسیل کا بیچنا اور راستہ سے گزرنے اور نالی سے پانی بہانے کا حق بیچنا ۔ پس اگر اول ہے تو دونوں مسئلوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ راستہ ایک معلوم چیز ہے۔ کیونکہ اس کا طول اور عرض معلوم ہے اور مسیل ایک مجہول چیز ہے کیونکہ یہ معلوم نہیں کہ پانی کس قدر جگہ گھیرے گا اور اگر ثانی ہے تو گذرنے کا حق بیچنے میں دو روایتیں ہیں اور ان دونوں میں سے ایک روایت پر
He said: The sale and gift of a pathway are permissible, and the sale and gift of a watercourse are invalid. The issue can be understood in two ways: (1) the sale of the land itself (raqaba) of the pathway and watercourse, or (2) the sale of the right of passage and the right to channel water. If it is the first, then the difference between the two cases is that the pathway is known (ma'lum) because it has known length and width, while the watercourse is unknown (majhul) because it is not known how much water it will occupy. If it is the second, then regarding the sale of the right of passage there are two narrations. The difference between it and the right to channel water, according to one of them, is that the right of passage is known because it is attached to a known location (the pathway), while the watercourse on a roof is analogous to haqq al-ta'li (right to build), and on the ground it is unknown due to the unknown location. The difference between the right of passage and haqq al-ta'li according to one of the two narrations is that haqq al-ta'li is attached to a non-permanent entity (the building), so it resembles usufruct (manafi'), while the right of passage is attached to a permanent entity (the land), so it resembles tangible property (a'yan). (Urdu commentary follows.)
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on selling and gifting a pathway (tariq) and a watercourse (masil). Selling and gifting a pathway is permissible, while selling and gifting a watercourse is invalid. The text then explains that the ruling can be interpreted in two ways: either the object of sale is the land itself (raqaba) of the pathway or watercourse, or it is the right of passage (haqq al-murur) and the right to channel water (haqq al-tasil). If it is the land itself, the difference is that a pathway is known (ma'lum) because it has known length and width, while a watercourse is unknown because the amount of water it will carry cannot be predicted. If it is the right of passage, there are two riwayat regarding its sale. The difference between the right of passage and the right to channel water is that the right of passage is attached to a permanent entity (the pathway), while the right to channel water on a roof is like haqq al-ta'li (attached to a non-permanent building), and on ground it is unknown due to the unknown location. The passage compares haqq al-ta'li with the right of passage: haqq al-ta'li is attached to a non-permanent structure (building), so it resembles usufruct (manafa'), while the right of passage is attached to permanent land, so it resembles tangible property (ayn).

Key Terms
طریق (tariq): A pathway with known length and width, considered a known (ma'lum) entity. Its sale and gift are permissible.
مسیل (masil): A watercourse for flowing water, considered unknown (majhul) because the amount of water it will carry is unpredictable. Its sale and gift are invalid.
حق مرور (haqq al-murur): The right of passage over a path. There are two narrations regarding its sale; one permits it because it is attached to a known location.
حق تسبيل (haqq al-tasil): The right to channel water through a watercourse. On a roof, it is analogous to haqq al-ta'li; on ground, it is unknown. Its sale is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason that selling a watercourse (masil) is invalid when the object of sale is the land itself?
A) Because watercourses are owned by the government.
B) Because a watercourse is unknown in the amount of water it will carry.
C) Because it is not permissible to sell rights.
D) Because the water may not be present.

Key Takeaway
The validity of selling a pathway or watercourse depends on whether the object is the land itself (known vs. unknown) or a right (permanence of attachment), with an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the right of passage.

Sharb as Property and Its Sale by Necessity
Establishes sharb as property and permits its sale even if water is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the Hanafi school considers sharb as property and explain the validity of its sale even when water is absent, using necessity and analogy with salam and istisna'.

Core Idea
Sharb is property (māl); its sale is valid even if water is absent due to necessity (ḍarūrah).

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes that the right to water (sharb) is a form of property that can be sold. It provides two proofs demonstrating that sharb has legal value, then addresses a critical objection: how can one sell water that may not exist at the time of contract? The answer, grounded in necessity and analogy with permissive sales of non-existent items, reveals a key application of istihsan in Hanafi sales law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and proofs that sharb is property
The passage first proves sharb is māl: (1) destroying another's sharb incurs liability (ḍamān), which only applies to property; (2) when sharb is reclaimed, the buyer receives a refund, showing it has a price. Conclude that sharb is property, so its sale is valid.
Focus on the two arguments: liability and price reduction.
2
Distinguish sharb from haqq al-taʿlī
The author contrasts sharb with the right of elevation (haqq al-taʿlī), which is not property and cannot be sold. Understand why: taʿlī attaches to non-permanent structures, unlike sharb which is a stable water right.
This distinction helps define what counts as māl in Hanafi law.
3
Understand the objection about water's absence
A questioner objects: if sharb is essentially water, selling it when water is absent should be invalid because selling a non-existent thing (maʿdūm) is forbidden.
The objection is based on the general prohibition of gharar.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response using necessity and analogy
The author answers that necessity (ḍarūrah) allows presuming the existence of water, just as in salam and istisnāʿ where the sold item is non-existent yet valid. This is an application of istihsan overriding the general rule.
Notice the illa: both involve a common need that legitimizes the sale of a non-existent but expected item.

Source Text

كتاب البيوع
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد هشتم مخصوص حصہ کو تلف کر دیا تو تلف کرنے والے پر اس کا ضمان واجب ہوگا اور تلف کی صورت یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے اپنی زمین کو دوسرے کے شرب سے سیراب کیا تو اس زمین کے مالک پر صاحب شرب کے لئے ضمان واجب ہو گا اور عمان اموال کو تلف کرنے سے لا زم ہونا ہے نہ کہ غیر اموال کو تلف کرنے سے پس ثابت ہوا کہ شرب یعنی پانی کا حصہ مال ہے نیز شرب کے مقابلہ میں شمن کا ایک حصہ ہوتا ہے۔ مثلاً ایک آدمی نے کسی سے اس کی زمین اور زمین کا شرب ایک ہزار روپیہ میں خریدا پھر اس کا شرب مستحق ہو گیا یعنی کسی تیسرے آدمی نے اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر کے شرب لے لیا تو مشتری سے مشرب کا حصہ ثمن کم کر دیا جائے گا اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ شرب کے مقابلہ میں شمن آتا ہے اور جس چیز کے مقابلہ میں شمن آتا ہو وہ چیز از قبیلہ مال ہوتی ہے پس ثابت ہوا کہ شرب مال ہے اور مال کی بیع جائز ہوتی ہے اس لئے شرب کی بیع جائز ہوگی اور رہا حق تعلی تو سابق میں گذر چکا کہ وہ مال نہیں ہے اس لئے اس کی بیع درست نہ ہوگی ۔ مسئلہ شرب کی پوری تفصیل کتاب الشرب میں ذکر کی جائے گی۔ فانتظروا انى معكم من المنتظرين سوال .... یہاں ایک سوال باقی رہ جاتا ہے وہ یہ کہ شرب کی بیع کو اس لئے جائز کہا گیا ہے کہ شرب پانی کے ایک حصہ کا نام ہے تو گویا بیچ پانی کی ہوئی اب اگر پانی کی گذرگاہ میں پانی موجود نہ ہو تو اس کی بیچ نا جائز ہونی چاہئے تھی کیونکہ معدوم کی بیع نا جائز ہوتی ہے حالانکہ اس کی بیع جائز ہے۔
جواب ضرورت کی وجہ سے پانی کے وجود کو فرض کر کے اس کی بیچ کو جائز قرار دے دیا گیا ہے جیسا کہ بیع سلم میں اور استصناع یعنی سائی دے کر کوئی چیز بنوانے میں مبیع معدوم ہوتی ہے لیکن اس کے باوجود بیع درست ہے پس اسی طرح اگر فی الوقت پانی موجود نہ ہو تو اس کا وجود فرض کر کے ضرورت کے پیش نظر شرب کی بیع کو جائز قرار دیا گیا ہے۔
The Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary volume 8: If a specific portion is destroyed, the one who destroys must guarantee it. The form of destruction is: a person irrigated his land with another's right to water (sharb); then the landowner is liable to the owner of the sharb. Liability is incurred by destroying property, not by destroying non-property. Thus it is proven that sharb—i.e., a share of water—is property. Also, in exchange for sharb, a portion of the price is given. For example, a person bought from someone his land and its sharb for one thousand rupees; then the sharb became claimed (i.e., a third person proved his right and took the sharb); then from the buyer the portion of the price corresponding to the sharb will be reduced. From this it is known that a price is given in exchange for sharb, and something for which a price is given is of the category of property. So sharb is property, and sale of property is valid; therefore sale of sharb is valid. As for the right of elevation (haqq al-ta'li), it has already passed that it is not property, so its sale is not valid. The full details of the issue of sharb will be mentioned in the Book of Sharb. 'So wait, indeed I am with you among those who wait.' Question: There remains a question: the sale of sharb is declared permissible because sharb is a name for a share of water, so it is essentially a sale of water. Now, if water is not present in its channel, then its sale should be invalid because sale of a non-existent thing is invalid, yet its sale is valid. Answer: Due to necessity, the existence of water is assumed and its sale is declared permissible, just as in salam sale and istisna' (ordering something to be made) the sold item is non-existent, yet the sale is valid. Similarly, if water is not present at the time, its existence is assumed and, considering necessity, the sale of sharb is declared permissible.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with its underlying reasoning on the nature and sale of sharb (right to water). The author first establishes that sharb is property (mal) by two proofs: (1) destruction of another's sharb entails liability (daman), which only applies to property; (2) when sharb is found to belong to someone else, the buyer gets a refund of a portion of the price, showing that sharb has a value. Therefore, selling sharb is valid. The passage then distinguishes sharb from haqq al-ta'li (right of elevation), which is not property and cannot be sold. After this, a question arises: if sharb is essentially water, and water may be absent at the time of sale, how can the sale be valid given that selling a non-existent thing (ma'dum) is generally prohibited? The answer invokes necessity (darurah): just as salam and istisna' contracts allow selling non-existent items due to common need, the sale of sharb is permitted by presuming the existence of water. This is a form of istihsan based on necessity.

Key Terms
شرب (sharb): The right to a share of water for irrigation, considered a form of property in this passage.
حق تعلی (haqq al-taʿlī): The right to elevate or build upward (air rights), which is not considered property and cannot be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the absence of water makes the contract a forward sale (salam) automatically.
D) Because the seller guarantees that water will appear, removing uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
Sharb is property (māl) and its sale is valid even when water is absent, due to necessity (ḍarūrah) justified by analogy with salam and istisna'.

Selling a Path vs. a Watercourse: Physical Objects and Rights
Distinguishes ruling on selling path vs. watercourse, physical vs. rights, with intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a physical path (ṭarīq) and a physical watercourse (masīl), and identify the two narrations regarding the sale of the right of way (ḥaqq al-murūr), explaining the reasoning (taʿlīl) based on knowledge (ʿilm) and permanence (baqāʾ).

Core Idea
A physical path is known, so its sale is valid; a watercourse is unknown, so its sale is void—but selling rights has two narrations.

This passage tackles a nuanced issue in Hanafi sales law: can you sell a path or a watercourse? The answer depends on what exactly you are selling—the physical ground or just the right to use it. The author of al-Hidayah distinguishes between selling the substance (raqabah) and selling a right (ḥaqq), and within rights, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible interpretations
The passage says the ruling on selling a path and a watercourse admits two possibilities: (1) selling the physical path/watercourse (raqabah), or (2) selling the right of way and right to drain water. First, confirm which interpretation is being discussed.
2
Analyse the ruling for physical objects
If we are selling the physical path (ṣubstantial path), what is its ruling? The passage says it is permissible. Why? Because a path is considered known (maʿlūm): its length and width are either specified or legally defined (e.g., width of the main door, length to the public road). Certainty in the subject matter prevents dispute. For the physical watercourse, the ruling is void. Why? Because the amount of water it will carry is unknown (majhūl), leading to uncertainty (gharar). But note: if the dimensions are specified, its sale becomes permissible (as per Qaḍī Khān).
The key legal principle: a sold item must be maʿlūm to avoid nizāʿ (dispute).
3
Examine the ruling for rights (ḥuqūq)
If the second interpretation is meant—selling the right of way (ḥaqq al-murūr) or the right to drain water (ḥaqq al-tasbīl)—then there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf. For the right of way, there are two narrations: one permits (Ibn Samāʿah) and one prohibits (al-Ziyādāt). The right to drain water is considered void in all narrations. Understand the reasoning behind these positions.
4
Understand the taʿlīl (legal cause) for the distinction between rights
According to the permissive narration (Ibn Samāʿah), why is selling the right of way permissible while selling the right to drain water is not? The passage explains: the right of way attaches to a permanent location (the ground), which remains over time, so it resembles physical assets (aʿyān). The right to drain water on a roof attaches to a non-permanent structure (the building), so it resembles usufructs (manāfiʿ), which are not property. On the ground, the right to drain water is unknown due to ignorance of its location.
The permanence (baqāʾ) of the location determines whether the right is akin to property.
5
Determine the muftā bihī (operative position)
The commentary indicates that Faqīh Abū al-Layth agrees with al-Ziyādāt, stating that rights alone cannot be sold. Therefore, the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is that selling the right of way (ḥaqq al-murūr) is not permissible. However, the text acknowledges two narrations, so the issue is not fully settled. For fatwā, one should follow the prohibition unless there is a clear need.
Authority weight: the ruling on physical objects is from Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah; the dispute on rights is from Nawādir.

كتاب البيوع
اشرف الهدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد هشتم اس میں اور پانی رواں ہونے کا حق بیچنے کے باطل ہونے میں فرق یہ ہے کہ راستہ سے گذرنے کا حق معلوم ہے کیونکہ اس کا تعلق ایک معلوم جگہ کے ساتھ ہے اور وہ راستہ ہے۔ رہا چھت پر سے پانی بہانے کا حق تو وہ حق تعلی کی نظیر ہے اور زمین پر مجہول ہے کیونکہ پانی بہنے کی جگہ مجہول ہے اور دو روایتوں میں سے ایک روایت پر حق مرور اور حق تعلق کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ حق تعلق ایسے مین کے ساتھ متعلق ہے جو باقی نہیں رہے گا۔ اور وہ نیچے کا مکان ہے پس یہ حق منافع کے مشابہ ہو گیا ہے اور رہا حق مرور تو وہ ایسے مین کے ساتھ متعلق ہے جو باقی رہتا ہے اور وہ زمین ہے پس یہ اعیان کے مشابہ ہو گیا۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ راستہ کا بیچنا اور اس کا ہبہ کرنا جائز ہے اور پانی رواں ہونے کی جگہ کا بچنا اور اس کا ہبہ کرنا ناجائز ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس مسئلہ کی دو صورتیں ہیں، ا۔ ایک مین طریق اور عین مسیل کا بیچنا جس پر سے انسان کا گذر ہوتا ہے اور جس پر پانی بہتا ہے گویا طریق سے مراد آدمی کی گذرگاہ اور مسیل سے مراد پانی کی گذرگاہ ہے۔ ۔ دوم یہ کہ طریق سے مراد حق مرور یعنی راستہ سے گذرنے کا حق اور مسیل سے مراد پانی بہانے کا حق بیچنا۔ اگر پہلی صورت ہو یعنی عین طریق اور گزرنے کی جگہ کا بیچنا تو جائز ہو اور عین مسیل یعنی پانی رواں ہونے کی جگہ کا بیچنانا جائز ہو۔
ان دونوں صورتوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے، کہ راستہ ایک معلوم چیز ہے کیونکہ راستہ کا طول اور عرض معلوم ہے۔ اگر راستہ کا طول و عرض بیان کر دیا گیا تب تو معلوم ہونا ظاہر ہے اور اگر بیان نہ کیا گیا تب بھی معلوم ہے کیونکہ راستہ کا طول و عرض شرعا مقدر ہے۔ بایں طور کہ راستہ کی چوڑائی گھر کے صدر دروازہ کی چوڑائی کے برابر ہوگی۔ اور راستہ کی لمبائی یہ ہوگی کہ وہ عام راستہ سے مل جائے ۔ پس جب راستہ یعنی گذرگاہ معلوم ہے اور مشاہد ومحسوس ہے تو اس میں کوئی نزاع واقع نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب کوئی نزاع واقع نہیں ہوگا تو بیع جائز ہے اور رہی پانی بہنے کی جگہ تو وہ مجہول ہے کیونکہ طول و عرض کے اعتبار سے یہ معلوم نہیں ہے کہ پانی کتنی جگہ گھیرے گا اور جب پانی بہنے کی جگہ کا طول و عرض معلوم نہیں تو مبیع مجہول ہوئی اور مجہول ہونے کی صورت میں بیچ نا جائز ہوتی ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں بیج نا جائز ہو گی ۔ لیکن یہ خیال رہے کہ بیچ کا جائز نہ ہونا اسی وقت تک ہے جب تک کہ پانی بہنے کی جگہ کا طول و عرض بیان نہ کیا گیا ہو اور پانی کے بہاؤ کے حدود اور اس کی جگہ معلوم ہو تو اس کی بیچ بھی جائز ہے۔ ( قاضی خاں)
اور اگر دوسری صورت مراد ہو یعنی طریق سے مراد وت مرور ہو اور مسیل سے مراد حق تسبیل ہو تو راستہ سے گذرنے کا حق بیچنے میں دو روایتیں ہیں۔ چنانچہ ابن سماعہ کی روایت میں حق مرور کا بیچنا جائز ہے اور زیادات کی روایت میں ناجائز ہے ۔ فقیہ ابواللیث کا قول بھی روایت زیادات کے موافق ہے چنانچہ فرمایا کہ حق مرور حقوق میں سے ایک حق ہے اور حقوق کی بیع تنہا جائز نہیں ہوتی اس لئے حق مرور کی بیع نا جائز ہے۔ اور پانی بہانے کے حق کی بیع کا جائز ہونا تمام روایات کے مطابق ہے پس زیادات کی روایت کے مطابق حق مرور کی بیع اور حق تسیل یعنی پانی بہانے کے حق کی بیع میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ دونوں نا جائز ہیں۔ البتہ ابن سماعہ کی روایت کے مطابق حق مرور کی بیع اور حق تسبیل کی بیچ میں فرق ہوگا کہ حق مرور کی بیع جائز اور حق تسبیل کی بیع نا جائز ہے ان دونوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ حق مرور یعنی راستہ سے گذرنے کا حق ایک امر معلوم ہے اور حق مرور امر معلوم اس لئے ہے کہ اس کا تعلق ایک معلوم جگہ کے ساتھ ہے اور وہ معلوم جگہ راستہ ہے جیسا کہ پہلے گزر چکا کہ راستہ کا طول و عراض بیان کرنے سے معلوم ہو جائے گا یا شریعت کے مقدر کرنے سے معلوم
The ruling on selling and gifting a path and a watercourse. He said: Selling a path and gifting it are permissible; selling a watercourse and gifting it are void. The issue admits two interpretations: selling the physical path/watercourse, and selling the right of way and right to drain water. If the first, then the face of distinction between the two issues is that the path is known because it has known length and width, whereas the watercourse is unknown because one does not know the amount of water it will occupy. If the second, then regarding the sale of the right of way there are two narrations. The face of distinction according to one of them between it and the right to drain water is that the right of way is known because it attaches to a known location, which is the path. As for the watercourse on the roof, it is analogous to the right of elevation (haqq al-ta'li), and on the ground it is unknown due to ignorance of its location. The face of distinction between the right of way and the right of elevation according to one of the two narrations is that the right of elevation attaches to a non-permanent entity, namely the building, so it resembles usufructs (manafi'). The right of way attaches to a permanent entity, namely the ground, so it resembles physical assets (a'yan). Translation: He said: Selling a path and gifting it are permissible; selling a watercourse and gifting it are void. This issue admits two possibilities: selling the substance of the path and watercourse, and selling the right of way and right to drain water. If the first, then the distinction between the two issues is that the path is known because its length and width are known, while the watercourse is unknown because it is not known how much space the water will occupy. If the second, then there are two narrations about selling the right of way. According to one of these two narrations, the distinction between it and the voidness of selling the right to drain water is that the right of way is known because it attaches to a known location—the path. As for the right to drain water from the roof, it is analogous to the right of elevation; on the ground it is unknown due to ignorance of its location. According to one of the two narrations, the distinction between the right of way and the right of elevation is that the right of elevation attaches to something that does not remain—the building—so it resembles usufructs. The right of way attaches to something that remains—the ground—so it resembles physical assets. Explanation of the issue: Selling a path and gifting it are permissible; selling a watercourse and gifting it are impermissible. The author of Hidayah says: This issue has two forms. One: selling the substance of the path and the watercourse—that upon which humans walk and upon which water flows. So by 'path' is meant the walking area, and by 'watercourse' is meant the water channel. Two: by 'path' is meant the right of way, and by 'watercourse' is meant the right to drain water. If the first form (selling the substance of the path and the walking area) then it is permissible; selling the substance of the watercourse (the water channel) is impermissible. The distinction between these two forms is that the path is a known thing because its length and width are known. If the length and width are stated, it is obviously known; if not stated, it is still known because the length and width are legally defined: the width equals the width of the main door of the house, and the length is that it connects to the public road. So when the path (the walking area) is known and perceptible, no dispute arises. If no dispute arises, the sale is valid. As for the water channel, it is unknown because its length and width are not known in terms of how much water it will occupy. When the dimensions are unknown, the sold item is unknown, and selling an unknown item is impermissible. Therefore, in this case the sale is impermissible. However, it should be noted that the sale is impermissible only as long as the dimensions of the water channel are not stated. If the boundaries of the water flow and its location are known, then its sale is also valid. (Qadhi Khan). And if the second form is intended—i.e., by 'path' is meant the right of way, and by 'watercourse' is meant the right to drain water—then regarding the sale of the right of way there are two narrations: in the narration of Ibn Sama'ah, the sale of the right of way is permissible; in the narration of al-Ziyadat, it is impermissible. The statement of Faqih Abu al-Layth also agrees with the narration of al-Ziyadat; he said that the right of way is one of the rights, and the sale of rights alone is not permissible, so the sale of the right of way is impermissible. The permissibility of selling the right to drain water is agreed upon in all narrations? Actually, according to the narration of al-Ziyadat, there is no difference between selling the right of way and selling the right to drain water—both are impermissible. However, according to the narration of Ibn Sama'ah, there is a difference: selling the right of way is permissible, while selling the right to drain water is impermissible. The face of distinction between them is that the right of way is a known matter because it attaches to a known location, which is the path. As mentioned before, the path becomes known by stating its length and width or by being legally quantified.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on selling and gifting a path (tariq) and a watercourse (masil). The author first presents the base ruling: selling a path is permissible, selling a watercourse is void. He then explains that the issue has two possible interpretations: either we are selling the physical substance (raqabah) of the path/watercourse, or we are selling the right of way (haqq al-murur) and the right to drain water (haqq al-tasbil). If the first interpretation, the distinction is that the path is known (ma'lum) in terms of length and width—either specified by contract or legally defined (e.g., width of the main door, length to the public road)—so the sale is valid. The watercourse is unknown (majhul) because the amount of water it will carry is not fixed, so its sale is void. However, if the watercourse's dimensions are specified, its sale becomes permissible (as noted from Qadhi Khan). If the second interpretation (selling rights), then there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to the narration of Ibn Sama'ah, selling the right of way is permissible; according to the narration of al-Ziyadat, it is impermissible. The right to drain water is impermissible in all narrations (except possibly?). The passage explains the reasoning: the right of way attaches to a permanent physical location (the ground), so it resembles property (a'yan), whereas the right to drain water on a roof resembles haqq al-ta'li (right of elevation), which attaches to a non-permanent structure and thus is like usufruct (manafi'), not property. The commentary then elaborates on the two forms and the legal definitions of a path's dimensions.

Key Terms
طريق (ṭarīq): A path or road used for walking; its physical substance (رقبة) is sold. It is considered known (معلم) either by specification or by legal definition.
مسیل (masīl): A watercourse or channel for water flow; its physical substance is unknown due to indeterminacy of water volume, so its sale is void unless dimensions are specified.
حق مرور (ḥaqq al-murūr): The right of way or passage over another's land. There are two narrations on its sale: permissible or prohibited.
حق تسبیل (ḥaqq al-tasbīl): The right to drain water (either from a roof or across land). Its sale is generally void.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is selling the physical substance of a watercourse (masīl) generally void?
A) Because water is impure and cannot be a commodity.
B) Because the dimensions of the watercourse are unknown (majhūl), causing potential dispute.
C) Because it is a public right and cannot be privately owned.
D) Because it is analogous to selling a usufruct (manfaʿah).

Key Takeaway
A physical path is known by legal definition, so its sale is valid; a physical watercourse is unknown due to indeterminate water volume, so its sale is void—and selling rights (especially the right of way) is subject to an intra-Hanafi dispute, with the relied-upon view being impermissible.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
8 min
Selling Physical Path vs. Physical Watercourse
Explains the first form: selling physical path vs physical watercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between selling a physical path and a physical watercourse, and state the ruling and conditions for each.

Core Idea
A physical path is known by Shari'ah-determined dimensions; a watercourse is unknown unless specified, making its sale invalid.

The author of al-Hidayah begins by clarifying two distinct forms of selling passage and water flow rights. The first form involves selling the physical path itself (the land where one walks) or the physical watercourse (the channel where water flows). Understanding why one is valid and the other is invalid hinges on the concept of knowledge (ma'lum) in the object of sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two physical forms
First, understand that we are discussing selling the actual path (tariq) or actual watercourse (masil), not the right to use them. The path is a tangible piece of land; the watercourse is also a tangible channel.
2
Analyze why the path is known
The path's length and width are either specified by the parties or, if unspecified, are determined by Shari'ah: width equals the width of the main door, length extends to the public road. Thus it is always known and dispute-free.
This knowledge (ma'lum) is a condition for valid sale.
3
Analyze why the watercourse is unknown
The watercourse's extent depends on water volume and cannot be precisely known in length and width. Unless its boundaries are explicitly defined, it remains unknown (majhul), leading to potential dispute.
Sale of an unknown object is impermissible (gharar).
4
State the rulings
Selling the physical path is permissible (ja'iz); selling the physical watercourse is impermissible (na'ja'iz) unless its dimensions and boundaries are clearly specified.
This ruling applies to the first form only.

بہانے کے حق کی بیع کا جائز ہونا تمام روایات کے مطابق ہے پس زیادات کی روایت کے مطابق حق مرور کی بیع اور حق تسبیل یعنی پانی بہانے کے حق کی بیع میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ دونوں نا جائز ہیں۔ البتہ ابن سماعہ کی روایت کے مطابق حق مرور کی بیع اور حق تسبیل کی بیع میں فرق ہوگا کہ حق مرور کی بیع جائز اور حق تسبیل کی بیع نا جائز ہے ان دونوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ حق مرور یعنی راستہ سے گذرنے کا حق ایک امر معلوم ہے اور حق مرور امر معلوم اس لئے ہے کہ اس کا تعلق ایک معلوم جگہ کے ساتھ ہے اور وہ معلوم جگہ راستہ ہے جیسا کہ پہلے گزر چکا کہ راستہ کا طول و عرض بیان کرنے سے معلوم ہو جائے گا یا شریعت کے مقدر کرنے سے معلوم ہوگا۔ بہر حال راستہ معلوم ہے اور جب راستہ معلوم ہے تو اس پر سے گزرنے کا حق بھی معلوم ہوگا اور جب حق مرور معلوم ہے تو اس کی بیع بھی جائز ہے۔ اور رہا حق تسبیل یعنی پانی بہنے کا حق تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں۔ پانی چھت پر بہتا ہوگا۔ یا زمین پر بہتا ہوگا اگر چھت پر بہتا ہے تو اس کی بیع دو جہوں سے نا جائز ہے ایک تو اس لئے کہ حق تسبیل کا تعلق ہوا سے ہے اور ہوا مال نہیں ہے پس اس صورت میں حق تسبیل حق تعلی کے مانند ہوگا اور چونکہ حق تعلی کی بیع نا جائز ہے اس لئے حق تسبیل کی بیع بھی نا جائز ہوگی۔ اور دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ حق تسبیل مجہول ہے کیونکہ پانی قلت اور کثرت کی وجہ سے پانی کا بہاؤ مختلف ہوگا پس تسبیل یعنی پانی کے مجہول ہونے کی وجہ سے بیع ناجائز ہوگی اور اگر پانی زمین پر بہتا ہے تو بھی تسبیل مجہول ہے کیونکہ پانی بہنے کی جگہ مجہول ہے۔ معلوم نہیں پانی کس قدر جگہ گھیرے گا اور جب تسبیل مجہول ہے تو اس کی بیع بھی ناجائز ہوگی ابن سماعہ کی روایت کے مطابق چونکہ حق مرور کی بیع جائز ہے اور سابق میں بیان ہوا کہ حق تعلی کی بیع نا جائز ہے ۔ اس لئے ان دونوں کے درمیان فرق کرنا بھی ضروری ہے سو وجہ فرق ملاحظہ فرمائیے فرماتے ہیں کہ حق تعلی کا تعلق ایسے عین کے ساتھ ہے جس کے لئے دائمی طور پر بقا نہیں ہے اور وہ عین نیچے کا مکان ہے پس حق تعلی منافع کے مشابہ ہو گیا وجہ شبہ دونوں کے لئے عدم بقاء ہے یعنی نہ منافع کے لئے بقاء ہے اور نہ مکان کے لئے بقاء ہے اور منافع کی بیع جائز نہیں ہوتی لہذا حق تعلی کی بیع بھی جائز نہ ہوگی اور رہا حق مرور تو اس کا تعلق ایسے عین کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے جس کے لئے دائمی طور پر بقاء ہے اور وہ عین زمین ہے پس حق مرور اعیان کے مشابہ ہو گیا اور وجہ شبہ دونوں کے لئے بقاء ہے یعنی جس طرح اعیان کے لئے بقاء ہے اسی طرح حق مرور کے لئے بقاء ہے اور اعیان کی بیع جائز ہے لہذا حق مرور کی بیع بھی جائز ہو گی۔ حاصل یہ کہ محل بیع یا تو وہ اعیان ہوں گے جو از قبیلہ اموال ہوں یا ایسا حق محل بیع ہو گا جو اعیان کے ساتھ متعلق ہو۔
In this, the difference between selling the right of water flow and its being invalid is that the right of passing through a path is known because it is connected to a known place, which is the path. As for the right of letting water flow from the roof, it is like the right of elevation [haqq al-ta'li] and is unknown on the ground because the place where water flows is unknown. And according to one of the two narrations, the reason for the difference between the right of passage and the right of elevation is that the right of elevation is connected to a thing that will not remain, which is the lower house. So this right resembles benefits (manafi'). As for the right of passage, it is connected to a thing that remains, which is the land, so it resembles physical assets (a'yan). The explanation of the issue is that selling a path and gifting it is permissible, and selling the place of water flow and gifting it is impermissible. The author of al-Hidayah says that this issue has two forms: First, selling the physical path and the physical watercourse, meaning the place where humans pass and where water flows. Second, selling the right of passage and the right of water flow. If it is the first form, selling the physical path is permissible and selling the physical watercourse is impermissible. The reason for the difference between these two forms is that the path is a known thing because its length and width are known. If the length and width are specified, it is obvious; if not specified, it is still known because the length and width are determined by Shari'ah: the width of the path equals the width of the main door of the house, and the length is that it connects to the public road. So when the path is known and observable, no dispute will occur, and when no dispute occurs, the sale is permissible. As for the place of water flow, it is unknown because its length and width are not known—how much space the water will occupy. Since the place of water flow is unknown in length and width, the sold item is unknown, and sale of an unknown item is impermissible. However, note that the impermissibility is only until the length and width of the place of water flow are specified; if the boundaries and location of the water flow are known, its sale is also permissible (Qadi Khan). And if the second form is meant, i.e., path means the right of passage and watercourse means the right of water flow (haqq al-tasbil), then there are two narrations regarding selling the right of passage: In the narration of Ibn Sama'a, selling the right of passage is permissible, and in the narration of al-Ziyadat, it is impermissible. The opinion of Faqih Abu al-Layth is also in accordance with the narration of al-Ziyadat; he said that the right of passage is one of the rights, and the sale of rights alone is not permissible, so the sale of the right of passage is impermissible. And the permissibility of selling the right of water flow is according to all narrations. So according to the narration of al-Ziyadat, there is no difference between selling the right of passage and the right of water flow, because both are impermissible. However, according to the narration of Ibn Sama'a, there will be a difference: selling the right of passage is permissible and selling the right of water flow is impermissible. The reason for the difference between these two is that the right of passage is a known matter because it is connected to a known place, which is the path. As mentioned earlier, the path becomes known by specifying its length and width or by Shari'ah determining it. In any case, the path is known, and when the path is known, the right of passing over it is also known. And when the right of passage is known, its sale is permissible. As for the right of water flow, it has two forms: water flows on the roof or on the ground. If it flows on the roof, its sale is impermissible from two aspects: first, because the right of water flow is connected to air, and air is not property, so this right resembles the right of elevation (haqq al-ta'li), and since the sale of the right of elevation is impermissible, so is the sale of the right of water flow. Second, because the right of water flow is unknown—the flow of water varies due to scarcity or abundance, so due to the unknown nature of the water flow, the sale is impermissible. And if water flows on the ground, the water flow is still unknown because the place where water flows is unknown; it is not known how much space the water will occupy. So when the water flow is unknown, its sale is also impermissible. According to the narration of Ibn Sama'a, since the sale of the right of passage is permissible and it was previously explained that the sale of the right of elevation is impermissible, it is necessary to differentiate between them. So observe the reason for the difference: They say that the right of elevation is connected to a thing that does not have permanent existence—the lower house. Thus the right of elevation resembles benefits (manafi'), because both lack permanence. And the sale of benefits is not permissible, so the sale of the right of elevation is also not permissible. As for the right of passage, it is connected to a thing that has permanent existence—the land itself. So the right of passage resembles physical assets (a'yan), because both have permanence. And the sale of physical assets is permissible, so the sale of the right of passage is also permissible. The conclusion is that the subject of sale is either physical assets that are of the category of property (amwal) or a right that is connected to physical assets.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling of selling two types of incorporeal rights: the right of passage (haqq al-murur) and the right of water flow (haqq al-tasbil). The author begins by distinguishing between selling the physical path (ayn al-tariq) and the physical watercourse (ayn al-masil). The physical path is known because its dimensions are either specified by the parties or determined by Shari'ah (width equal to the main door, length until it meets a public road). Therefore, its sale is valid. The physical watercourse is unknown because its extent depends on water volume and cannot be precisely known; thus its sale is invalid unless its boundaries are explicitly defined. The passage then addresses the second form: selling the rights themselves. For the right of passage, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: according to the narration of Ibn Sama'a (from al-Asl), it is permissible because it is known and connected to a known place; according to the narration of al-Ziyadat (by Abu Yusuf), it is impermissible because rights are not independently owned property. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is the permissibility of selling the right of passage, as it is associated with a permanent asset (land) and thus resembles the sale of the asset itself. The right of water flow, whether on a roof or ground, is generally impermissible to sell because it is either connected to non-property (air) or is unknown in extent. The reasoning uses qiyas: right of passage is like a'yan (permanent), right of water flow is like manafi' (temporary benefits) or like haqq al-ta'li (right of elevation). The author concludes by stating the general principle: the subject of sale must be either physical assets (a'yan) that are property, or a right attached to such assets.

Key Terms
عین طریق (ʿayn al-ṭarīq): The physical path or passageway itself, as a tangible piece of land.
عین مسیل (ʿayn al-masīl): The physical watercourse or channel where water flows.
معلوم (maʿlūm): Known; a condition for valid sale in Islamic law to avoid gharar.
مجہول (majhūl): Unknown; sale of a majhul item is invalid due to gharar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Hidayah, what is the default ruling for selling a physical watercourse (ayn al-masil) if its dimensions are not specified?
A) It is valid because water is property.
B) It is invalid because it is unknown.
C) It is valid if the price is low.
D) It is invalid only if there is a dispute.

Key Takeaway
A physical path is always known by Shari'ah estimation, making its sale valid; a physical watercourse is unknown unless specified, making its sale invalid by default.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Selling Right of Passage vs. Right of Water Flow
Explains the second form: selling the right of passage and right of water flow, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on selling the right of passage, and distinguish it from selling the right of water flow.

Core Idea
Selling right of passage is permissible (Ibn Sama'ah, mufta bihi) because it is known and attached to land; selling right of water flow is impermissible.

The author now turns to the second form: selling the intangible rights themselves. Within the Hanafi school, there is a well-known difference of opinion regarding the sale of the right of passage (haqq al-murur). By understanding the reasoning behind each narration, you will see how the school reconciles different transmissions and arrives at the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations
Ibn Sama'ah's narration from al-Asl: selling the right of passage is permissible (ja'iz). The narration of al-Ziyadat: it is impermissible (na'ja'iz). Faqih Abu al-Layth also follows al-Ziyadat.
Both are Zahir al-Riwayah, but the mufta bihi is the first.
2
Understand the reasoning for permissibility
Right of passage is known (its extent is determined by the path's dimensions) and it is attached to land, which is a permanent asset. Thus it resembles physical assets (a'yan) whose sale is valid.
This is a qiyas khafi: the illa is permanence of the underlying asset.
3
Understand the reasoning for impermissibility
Rights are not property (mal) and cannot be sold independently. The right of passage is just one of many rights; selling it alone is like selling a benefit, which is not allowed.
Al-Ziyadat and Abu al-Layth emphasize the non-property nature of rights.
4
Analyze the distinction from right of elevation
Right of elevation (haqq al-ta'li) is attached to a lower house that will not remain; thus it resembles benefits (manafi') and its sale is invalid. Right of passage is attached to land that remains; thus it resembles a'yan and its sale is valid.
The key is whether the underlying asset is permanent (baqi) or not.
5
Conclude with the right of water flow
Selling the right of water flow (haqq al-tasbil) is impermissible in all narrations. Whether the water flows on a roof or on the ground, it is either attached to air (non-property) or unknown in extent.
No dispute on this part.

ان دونوں صورتوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے، کہ راستہ ایک معلوم چیز ہے کیونکہ راستہ کا طول اور عرض معلوم ہے۔ اگر راستہ کا طول و عرض بیان کر دیا گیا تب تو معلوم ہونا ظاہر ہے اور اگر بیان نہ کیا گیا تب بھی معلوم ہے کیونکہ راستہ کا طول و عرض شرعا مقدر ہے۔ بایں طور کہ راستہ کی چوڑائی گھر کے صدر دروازہ کی چوڑائی کے برابر ہوگی۔ اور راستہ کی لمبائی یہ ہوگی کہ وہ عام راستہ سے مل جائے ۔ پس جب راستہ یعنی گذرگاہ معلوم ہے اور مشاہد ومحسوس ہے تو اس میں کوئی نزاع واقع نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب کوئی نزاع واقع نہیں ہوگا تو بیع جائز ہے اور رہی پانی بہنے کی جگہ تو وہ مجہول ہے کیونکہ طول و عرض کے اعتبار سے یہ معلوم نہیں ہے کہ پانی کتنی جگہ گھیرے گا اور جب پانی بہنے کی جگہ کا طول و عرض معلوم نہیں تو مبیع مجہول ہوئی اور مجہول ہونے کی صورت میں بیع ناجائز ہوتی ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں بیع ناجائز ہو گی ۔ لیکن یہ خیال رہے کہ بیع کا جائز نہ ہونا اسی وقت تک ہے جب تک کہ پانی بہنے کی جگہ کا طول و عرض بیان نہ کیا گیا ہو اور پانی کے بہاؤ کے حدود اور اس کی جگہ معلوم ہو تو اس کی بیع بھی جائز ہے۔ ( قاضی خاں)
اور اگر دوسری صورت مراد ہو یعنی طریق سے مراد حق مرور ہو اور مسیل سے مراد حق تسبیل ہو تو راستہ سے گذرنے کا حق بیچنے میں دو روایتیں ہیں۔ چنانچہ ابن سماعہ کی روایت میں حق مرور کا بیچنا جائز ہے اور زیادات کرنے سے معلوم ہوگا۔ بہر حال راستہ معلوم ہے اور جب راستہ معلوم ہے تو اس پر سے گزرنے کا حق بھی معلوم ہوگا اور جب حق مرور معلوم ہے تو اس کی بیع بھی جائز ہے۔ اور رہا حق تسبیل یعنی پانی بہنے کا حق تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں۔ پانی چھت پر بہتا ہوگا۔ یا زمین پر بہتا ہوگا اگر چھت پر بہتا ہے تو اس کی بیع دو جہوں سے نا جائز ہے ایک تو اس لئے کہ حق تسبیل کا تعلق ہوا سے ہے اور ہوا مال نہیں ہے پس اس صورت میں حق تسبیل حق تعلی کے مانند ہوگا اور چونکہ حق تعلی کی بیع نا جائز ہے اس لئے حق تسبیل کی بیع بھی نا جائز ہوگی۔ اور دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ حق تسبیل مجہول ہے کیونکہ پانی قلت اور کثرت کی وجہ سے پانی کا بہاؤ مختلف ہوگا پس تسبیل یعنی پانی کے مجہول ہونے کی وجہ سے بیع ناجائز ہوگی اور اگر پانی زمین پر بہتا ہے تو بھی تسبیل مجہول ہے کیونکہ پانی بہنے کی جگہ مجہول ہے۔ معلوم نہیں پانی کس قدر جگہ گھیرے گا اور جب تسبیل مجہول ہے تو اس کی بیع بھی ناجائز ہوگی ابن سماعہ کی روایت کے مطابق چونکہ حق مرور کی بیع جائز ہے اور سابق میں بیان ہوا کہ حق تعلی کی بیع نا جائز ہے ۔ اس لئے ان دونوں کے درمیان فرق کرنا بھی ضروری ہے سو وجہ فرق ملاحظہ فرمائیے فرماتے ہیں کہ حق تعلی کا تعلق ایسے عین کے ساتھ ہے جس کے لئے دائمی طور پر بقا نہیں ہے اور وہ عین نیچے کا مکان ہے پس حق تعلی منافع کے مشابہ ہو گیا وجہ شبہ دونوں کے لئے عدم بقاء ہے یعنی نہ منافع کے لئے بقاء ہے اور نہ مکان کے لئے بقاء ہے اور منافع کی بیع جائز نہیں ہوتی لہذا حق تعلی کی بیع بھی جائز نہ ہوگی اور رہا حق مرور تو اس کا تعلق ایسے عین کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے جس کے لئے دائمی طور پر بقاء ہے اور وہ عین زمین ہے پس حق مرور اعیان کے مشابہ ہو گیا اور وجہ شبہ دونوں کے لئے بقاء ہے یعنی جس طرح اعیان کے لئے بقاء ہے اسی طرح حق مرور کے لئے بقاء ہے اور اعیان کی بیع جائز ہے لہذا حق مرور کی بیع بھی جائز ہو گی۔ حاصل یہ کہ محل بیع یا تو وہ اعیان ہوں گے جو از قبیلہ اموال ہوں یا ایسا حق محل بیع ہو گا جو اعیان کے ساتھ متعلق ہو۔
In this, the difference between selling the right of water flow and its being invalid is that the right of passing through a path is known because it is connected to a known place, which is the path. As for the right of letting water flow from the roof, it is like the right of elevation [haqq al-ta'li] and is unknown on the ground because the place where water flows is unknown. And according to one of the two narrations, the reason for the difference between the right of passage and the right of elevation is that the right of elevation is connected to a thing that will not remain, which is the lower house. So this right resembles benefits (manafi'). As for the right of passage, it is connected to a thing that remains, which is the land, so it resembles physical assets (a'yan). The explanation of the issue is that selling a path and gifting it is permissible, and selling the place of water flow and gifting it is impermissible. The author of al-Hidayah says that this issue has two forms: First, selling the physical path and the physical watercourse, meaning the place where humans pass and where water flows. Second, selling the right of passage and the right of water flow. If it is the first form, selling the physical path is permissible and selling the physical watercourse is impermissible. The reason for the difference between these two forms is that the path is a known thing because its length and width are known. If the length and width are specified, it is obvious; if not specified, it is still known because the length and width are determined by Shari'ah: the width of the path equals the width of the main door of the house, and the length is that it connects to the public road. So when the path is known and observable, no dispute will occur, and when no dispute occurs, the sale is permissible. As for the place of water flow, it is unknown because its length and width are not known—how much space the water will occupy. Since the place of water flow is unknown in length and width, the sold item is unknown, and sale of an unknown item is impermissible. However, note that the impermissibility is only until the length and width of the place of water flow are specified; if the boundaries and location of the water flow are known, its sale is also permissible (Qadi Khan). And if the second form is meant, i.e., path means the right of passage and watercourse means the right of water flow (haqq al-tasbil), then there are two narrations regarding selling the right of passage: In the narration of Ibn Sama'a, selling the right of passage is permissible, and in the narration of al-Ziyadat, it is impermissible. The opinion of Faqih Abu al-Layth is also in accordance with the narration of al-Ziyadat; he said that the right of passage is one of the rights, and the sale of rights alone is not permissible, so the sale of the right of passage is impermissible. And the permissibility of selling the right of water flow is according to all narrations. So according to the narration of al-Ziyadat, there is no difference between selling the right of passage and the right of water flow, because both are impermissible. However, according to the narration of Ibn Sama'a, there will be a difference: selling the right of passage is permissible and selling the right of water flow is impermissible. The reason for the difference between these two is that the right of passage is a known matter because it is connected to a known place, which is the path. As mentioned earlier, the path becomes known by specifying its length and width or by Shari'ah determining it. In any case, the path is known, and when the path is known, the right of passing over it is also known. And when the right of passage is known, its sale is permissible. As for the right of water flow, it has two forms: water flows on the roof or on the ground. If it flows on the roof, its sale is impermissible from two aspects: first, because the right of water flow is connected to air, and air is not property, so this right resembles the right of elevation (haqq al-ta'li), and since the sale of the right of elevation is impermissible, so is the sale of the right of water flow. Second, because the right of water flow is unknown—the flow of water varies due to scarcity or abundance, so due to the unknown nature of the water flow, the sale is impermissible. And if water flows on the ground, the water flow is still unknown because the place where water flows is unknown; it is not known how much space the water will occupy. So when the water flow is unknown, its sale is also impermissible. According to the narration of Ibn Sama'a, since the sale of the right of passage is permissible and it was previously explained that the sale of the right of elevation is impermissible, it is necessary to differentiate between them. So observe the reason for the difference: They say that the right of elevation is connected to a thing that does not have permanent existence—the lower house. Thus the right of elevation resembles benefits (manafi'), because both lack permanence. And the sale of benefits is not permissible, so the sale of the right of elevation is also not permissible. As for the right of passage, it is connected to a thing that has permanent existence—the land itself. So the right of passage resembles physical assets (a'yan), because both have permanence. And the sale of physical assets is permissible, so the sale of the right of passage is also permissible. The conclusion is that the subject of sale is either physical assets that are of the category of property (amwal) or a right that is connected to physical assets.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling of selling two types of incorporeal rights: the right of passage (haqq al-murur) and the right of water flow (haqq al-tasbil). The author begins by distinguishing between selling the physical path (ayn al-tariq) and the physical watercourse (ayn al-masil). The physical path is known because its dimensions are either specified by the parties or determined by Shari'ah (width equal to the main door, length until it meets a public road). Therefore, its sale is valid. The physical watercourse is unknown because its extent depends on water volume and cannot be precisely known; thus its sale is invalid unless its boundaries are explicitly defined. The passage then addresses the second form: selling the rights themselves. For the right of passage, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: according to the narration of Ibn Sama'a (from al-Asl), it is permissible because it is known and connected to a known place; according to the narration of al-Ziyadat (by Abu Yusuf), it is impermissible because rights are not independently owned property. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is the permissibility of selling the right of passage, as it is associated with a permanent asset (land) and thus resembles the sale of the asset itself. The right of water flow, whether on a roof or ground, is generally impermissible to sell because it is either connected to non-property (air) or is unknown in extent. The reasoning uses qiyas: right of passage is like a'yan (permanent), right of water flow is like manafi' (temporary benefits) or like haqq al-ta'li (right of elevation). The author concludes by stating the general principle: the subject of sale must be either physical assets (a'yan) that are property, or a right attached to such assets.

Key Terms
حق المرور (ḥaqq al-murūr): The right to pass through a specific path or land belonging to another.
حق التسبیل (ḥaqq al-tasbīl): The right to let water flow across another's property, either from a roof or on the ground.
حق التعلی (ḥaqq al-taʿlī): The right to construct an upper floor or elevation over another's property.
عین (ʿayn): A physical, tangible asset that is property (māl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling the right of passage (haqq al-murur)?
A) It is invalid because rights are not property.
B) It is valid because it is known and attached to land.
C) It is valid only if the land is also sold.
D) It is invalid because it is unknown.

Key Takeaway
The right of passage is saleable because it is known and attached to permanent land (like 'ayan); the right of water flow is not, because it is unknown or attached to air.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Mistaken Gender in Sale: Humans vs. Animals
Ruling on gender mistake distinguished by human vs animal jins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of jins (species) to determine whether a mistake in gender voids a sale or grants an option.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a slave-girl that turns out to be male voids the sale; a ram that turns out to be a ewe is valid with an option.

This lesson examines a classic ruling from al-Hidayah that illustrates a key distinction in Hanafi contract law: when the described gender of a sold item is incorrect, the outcome depends on whether the item is human or animal. The underlying principle, drawn from the Book of Nikah by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, governs how contracts interact with indication (isharah) and description (tasmiyah). By understanding the role of 'species' (jins) and 'purposes' (aghrad), you will be able to predict the legal effect of such mistakes in sale transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two case scenarios
The passage presents two contrasting cases: buying a slave-girl who turns out to be male, and buying a ram that turns out to be a ewe. Note the different rulings immediately stated.
The matn uses 'fala bay' (no sale) for the human case and 'yan'qid wa yatakhayyar' (concluded with option) for the animal case.
2
Recognize the different outcomes
For the human case, the sale is void (batil). For the animal case, the sale is valid (sahih) but the buyer has a khiyar (option) due to the missing description.
The khiyar here is khiyar al-wasf (option for missing attribute), not khiyar al-ayb (defect).
3
Understand the principle of isharah ma'a al-tasmiyah
The commentary explains that when both physically pointing to an object (isharah) and verbally describing it (tasmiyah) are present, the contract attaches to the described species if the species of the indicated object differs. If the described species is absent, the contract is void. If the species matches, the contract attaches to the indicated object, and the buyer gets an option if a specific quality is missing.
This principle is derived from the Book of Nikah by Imam Muhammad, showing that the rule applies across contracts.
4
Apply the jins distinction: humans vs. animals
In humans, male and female are considered two separate species (jinsan) because their purposes (aghrad) are vastly different. In animals, male and female are one species because their purposes are similar (e.g., both can be used for meat, breeding, etc.). Therefore, a gender mistake in humans voids the contract (the described species is absent), while in animals the contract is valid (the species is present) but the buyer has an option for the missing description of gender.
The passage analogizes to vinegar and date syrup being different species despite both being liquids, showing that jins is defined by purpose, not by form.
5
Trace the qiyas to the baker/scribe example
The commentary compares the animal case to buying a slave described as a baker who turns out to be a scribe: same species, different attribute. In both, the sale is valid with khiyar. The illa (effective cause) is that the mistake concerns an attribute within the same species, not a different species.
This qiyas reinforces that khiyar al-wasf is appropriate when the species is correct but a specific quality is missing.

باندی بیچی وہ غلام نکلا اور مینڈھا بیچا وہ بھیڑ نکلی
قال ومن باع جارية فاذا هو غلام فلا بيع بينهما بخلاف ما اذا باع كبشا فاذا هو نعجة حيث ينعقد البيع ويتخير والفرق يبتنى على الاصل الذي ذكرناه فى النكاح لمحمد وهو ان الاشارة مع التسمية اذا اجتمعتا في مختلفى الجنس يتعلق العقد بالمسمى ويبطل لانعدامه وفى متحدى الجنس يتعلق بالمشار اليه وينعقد لوجوده ويتخير لفوات الوصف كمن اشترى عبدا على انه خباز فاذا هو كاتب وفي مسألتنا الذكر والانثى من بنی آدم جنسان للتفاوت فى الاغراض وفى الحيوانات جنس واحد للتقارب فيها وهو المعتبر في بنی آدم [؟] كالخل والدبس جنسان والتمر والزبيب [؟] جنسان مع اتحاد أصلهما
ترجمہ کہا اور جس نے باندی فروخت کی پھر وہ غلام نکلا تو ان دونوں کے درمیان بیع نہیں ہے برخلاف اس کے جبکہ کسی نے مینڈھا فروخت کیا لیکن وہ بھیڑ نکلی تو بیع منعقد ہو جائے گی اور مشتری کو اختیار ہو گا اور فرق اس اصل پر مبنی ہے جسے ہم نے کتاب النکاح میں امام محمد کی طرف منسوب کر کے بیان کیا ہے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ اشارہ مع التسمیہ جب جمع ہو جائیں تو جنس مختلف ہونے کی صورت میں عقد مسمی کے ساتھ متعلق ہوگا اور اس کے معدوم ہونے سے عقد باطل ہو جائے گا اور جنس متحد ہونے کی صورت میں عقد کا تعلق مشار الیہ کے ساتھ ہو گا اور اس کے موجود ہونے سے عقد منعقد ہو جائے گا اور وصف کے فوت ہونے کی وجہ سے مشتری کو اختیار ہوگا جیسے کسی نے ایک غلام اس شرط پر خریدا کہ وہ روٹی پکانے والا ہے مگر وہ لکھنے والا نکلا۔ اور ہمارے مسئلہ مذکورہ میں بنو آدم کے نر و مادہ دو جنس ہیں کیونکہ ان کی اغراض میں تفاوت ہے اور جانوروں میں نر و مادہ ایک جنس ہیں کیونکہ ان کی اغراض میں تقارب ہے اور اختلاف جنس اور اتحاد جنس
He sold a slave-girl and it turned out to be a slave; and he sold a ram and it turned out to be a ewe. He said: And whoever sold a slave-girl and it turned out to be a slave, there is no sale between them, contrary to if he sold a ram and it turned out to be a ewe, where the sale is concluded and he has an option (khiyar). The difference is based on the principle we mentioned in the Book of Nikah attributed to Muhammad (Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani): that when indication (isharah) together with naming (tasmiyah) are combined, if the gender is different, the contract is attached to the named (musamma) and becomes void due to its non-existence; and if the gender is the same, it is attached to the indicated (mushar ilayh) and is concluded due to its existence, and he has an option due to the loss of the description. Like one who bought a slave on condition that he is a baker, and he turns out to be a scribe. In our issue, male and female of the children of Adam are two species due to the difference in purposes (aghrad), and in animals they are one species due to the similarity in purposes. And this is considered in the children of Adam [؟] like vinegar and date syrup are two species, and dates and raisins [؟] are two species despite the unity of their origin. Translation: He said: And whoever sold a slave-girl and it turned out to be a slave, there is no sale between them, contrary to if someone sold a ram and it turned out to be a ewe, then the sale is concluded and the buyer has an option. The difference is based on the principle we mentioned in the Book of Nikah attributed to Imam Muhammad: that when indication together with naming are combined, in the case of different genders, the contract is attached to the named and becomes void due to its non-existence; in the case of same gender, it is attached to the indicated and is concluded due to its existence, and he has an option due to the loss of the description, like one who bought a slave on condition that he is a baker and he turns out to be a scribe. In our mentioned issue, male and female of the children of Adam are two species because their purposes differ, and in animals male and female are one species because their purposes are similar. And difference of species and unity of species.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the effect of mistaken gender in a sale. The matn states that if a person sells a slave-girl and it turns out to be a male slave, the sale is void (no sale between them). However, if someone sells a ram (male sheep) and it turns out to be a ewe (female sheep), the sale is valid (concluded) but the buyer has an option (khiyar) due to the missing description. The sharh (commentary) explains the underlying principle derived from the Book of Nikah by Imam Muhammad. When a contract involves both an indication (pointing to a specific object) and a verbal description (naming the species or type), the contract attaches to the description if the indicated object differs in species from the description; if the described species does not exist in the object, the contract is void. If they are the same species, the contract attaches to the indicated object and is valid, but the buyer has an option if a specific quality (like being a baker) is missing. The reasoning applies here: in humans, male and female are considered different species (jinsān) because the purposes (aghrad) for buying a male slave differ greatly from those for a female slave. In animals, however, male and female are the same species because the purposes are similar (both for meat, breeding, etc.). Therefore, selling a female human slave that turns out to be male voids the contract, while selling a ram that turns out to be a ewe is valid with an option for the buyer. The author also notes parallels with other goods: vinegar and date syrup are different species despite both being liquids, and dates and raisins are also different species despite both being dried fruits from trees.

Key Terms
جنس (jins (pl. ajnās)): A category of things that share the same legal essence for the purpose of contract; used to determine whether a mistake in description voids the sale or only grants an option.
الاشارة مع التسمية (al-ishārah maʿa al-tasmiyah): The combination of pointing to a specific object (ishārah) and describing it by name or type (tasmiyah) in a contract.
خيار (khiyār): Option or right to rescind or confirm a contract upon discovering a defect or missing attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person sells a ewe (female sheep) and it turns out to be a ram (male sheep)?
A) The sale is void because male and female are different species.
B) The sale is valid but the buyer has an option.
C) The sale is valid and the buyer has no option because it is the same species.
D) The sale is void unless the buyer agrees.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between humans and animals in cases of mistaken gender because male and female are different species (jins) in humans but one species in animals, based on the legal consideration of purposes (aghrad).

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Selling a Slave vs. Animal with Gender Misidentification
Distinguishes ruling when sold slave's gender differs from named vs animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ishara and tasmiya to determine whether a sale is valid or void when the pointed-to item differs from the named description.

Core Idea
In sale, if pointed-to and named belong to different jins, the sale is void; if same jins, sale valid with option.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling: when a seller points to a slave-girl but it turns out to be a male slave, the sale is void. Yet if a ram turns out to be a ewe, the sale is valid with an option. The difference hinges on a juristic principle about categories (jins) and the interplay of pointing (ishara) and naming (tasmiya). Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to analyze sale contracts where description and reality diverge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios and their rulings
The passage gives two cases: (1) selling a slave-girl who turns out to be a male → no sale (batil); (2) selling a ram who turns out to be a ewe → sale is concluded (sahih) with an option (khiyar). Note the contrast.
The ruling for the human case is void; for the animal case, valid with option.
2
Locate the underlying principle (asl)
The principle is taken from Nikah (marriage) by Imam Muhammad: when ishara (pointing) and tasmiya (naming) are both present, if the pointed-to and named are of different categories, the contract attaches to the named; if same category, it attaches to the pointed-to.
This principle is analogically applied to sales.
3
Apply the principle via qiyas
The far' (new case) is the sale scenario. The illa (effective cause) is whether the two belong to the same or different jins (juristic category). The asl is the Nikah case. The hukm is transferred accordingly.
The type of qiyas is jaliyyah; the illa is extracted by tanqih al-manat.
4
Determine jins for humans and animals
In the Hanafi school, human male and female are different jins because their purposes (aghrad) differ significantly (e.g., one for domestic service, another for outdoor work). For animals, male and female serve similar purposes (meat, riding, burden), so they are one jins.
The criterion is aghrad, not biological origin.
5
Conclusion: void vs. valid with option
For humans (different jins): contract attaches to the named (female slave), which is non-existent → sale void. For animals (same jins): contract attaches to the pointed-to (ewe), which exists → sale valid; but the missing description (ram) gives the buyer an option to cancel.
The option is khiyar al-‘ayb (option due to defect/description loss).

قال ومن باع جارية فاذا هو غلام فلا بيع بينهما بخلاف ما اذا باع كبشا فاذا هو نعجة حيث ينعقد البيع ويتخير والفرق يبتنى على الاصل الذي ذكرناه فى النكاح لمحمد وهوان الاشارة مع التسمية اذا اجتمعتا في مختلفى الجنس يتعلق العقد بالمسمى ويبطل لا نعدامه وفى متحدى الجنس يتعلق بالمشار اليه وينعقد لو جوده ويتخير لفوات الوصف كمن اشترى عبدا على انه خباز فاذا هو كاتب وفي مسألتنا الذكر والانثى من بنی آدم جنسان للتفاوت فى الاغراض وفى الحيوانات جنس واحد للتقارب فيها وهو المعتبر في نیادم هدادون الأصل كالخل والدبس جنسان والوذارى والزند نيجى على ما قالو اجنسان مع اتحاد أصلهما
He said: Whoever sells a slave-girl and then it turns out to be a male slave, there is no sale between them. Unlike if he sells a ram and then it turns out to be a ewe, where the sale is concluded and he has an option. The difference is based on the principle we mentioned in Nikah from Muhammad: that when pointing and naming come together, if the two are of different categories, the contract attaches to the named (musamma) and is invalid due to its non-existence. If the categories are the same, the contract attaches to the pointed-to (mushar ilayh) and is concluded and he has an option due to loss of description, like one who buys a slave on condition that he is a baker and then he is a scribe. In our issue, male and female among humans are two categories due to difference in purposes; among animals they are one category due to similarity. And it is this that is considered, not the origin being the same or different, like vinegar and grape syrup are two categories, and Wadhari and Zandani cloth are two categories according to what they said, despite their origin being the same.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It addresses the scenario where a sold slave-girl turns out to be a male, and contrasts it with selling a ram that turns out to be a ewe. The ruling: in the human case, the sale is void; in the animal case, the sale is valid but the buyer has an option (khiyar al-‘ayb). The underlying principle, attributed to Imam Muhammad, is that when the seller both points to the item and names it, the contract attaches to the named if the two (pointed-to and named) belong to different juristic categories. If the categories are the same, it attaches to the pointed-to. Among humans, male and female are considered different categories because the purposes (aghrad) sought from each differ significantly (e.g., a male for outdoor work, a female for household service). Among animals, male and female serve similar purposes (meat, riding, burden), so they are one category. Thus, pointing to a male but naming a female (slave-girl) means the named (female) is nonexistent, so the sale is void. Pointing to a ewe but naming a ram (both animals) means the pointed-to (ewe) exists, so the sale is valid; the buyer can cancel due to missing description. The ruling is based on this categorization, not on physical origin. Examples: vinegar and grape syrup share origin but are two categories; similarly, two types of cloth (Wadhari and Zandani) considered two categories by agreement of scholars. The Hanafi position is clear: human genders are distinct categories; animal genders are not.

Key Terms
اشارة (ishara): Pointing to the specific item being sold (the 'pointed-to' object).
تسمية (tasmiya): Naming or describing the item being sold (e.g., saying 'I sell this slave-girl').
جنس (jins): A juristic category or kind, not necessarily biological; determined by similarity of purposes (aghrad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of a slave-girl who turns out to be a male void, while the sale of a ram that turns out to be a ewe is valid with an option?
A) Because human male and female are considered the same juristic category
B) Because human male and female are considered different juristic categories due to differing purposes
C) Because the pointed-to item is always the male
D) Because the seller did not intend to sell the animal

Key Takeaway
The validity of a sale where the described item differs from the pointed-to item depends on whether the two belong to the same juristic category (jins), determined by purposes (aghrad) rather than biological or physical origin.
