وہ شرط جس کا عقد تقاضہ نہیں کرتا لیکن اس میں کسی کا کوئی نفع بھی نہیں ہے۔ جیسے بائع کا یہ شرط لگانا کہ مشتری خریدے ہوئے جانور کو فروخت نہ کرے۔ اس صورت میں ظاہر مذہب یہ ہے کہ بیع صحیح ہو جائے گی لیکن شرط لغو ہوگی یعنی شرط کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس شرط کی وجہ سے مبیع یعنی جانور مطالبہ نہیں کرے گا اور مطالبہ اس لئے نہیں کرے گا کہ وہ استحقاق مطالبہ کا اہل نہیں ہے اور جب مطالبہ نہیں کرے گا تو ربا کے معنی بھی لازم نہ آئیں گے۔ اور نہ کوئی جھگڑا واقع ہوگا حالانکہ یہی دو باتیں مفسد بیع تھی پس جب دونوں باتیں نہیں پائی گئیں تو بیع فاسد بھی نہ ہوگی ۔ امام ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے کہ اس صورت میں بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی ۔
Because in it there is an increase devoid of consideration, so it leads to riba, or because dispute occurs due to it, so the contract becomes devoid of its purpose, unless it is customary, for custom overrides analogy. And if the contract does not necessitate it and there is no benefit in it for anyone, it does not invalidate it, and that is the apparent position of the school, such as the condition that the buyer not sell the purchased animal, because the demand is nonexistent, so it does not lead to riba or dispute.

Translation: And if someone sold his slave with the condition that the buyer emancipate him, or make him mudabbar, or make him mukatab, or sold a slave-girl with the condition that the buyer make her umm walad (in all these cases) the sale is fasid (voidable) because it is a sale and a condition, whereas the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited sale and condition. Then the summarized position of the school is that every condition that the contract does not necessitate, like stipulating ownership for the buyer, does not invalidate the contract because the buyer's ownership is established without the condition. And every condition that the contract does not necessitate and in which there is a benefit for the seller or buyer or the subject matter, provided the subject matter is eligible for claiming (i.e., a human), then that condition invalidates the contract, such as the condition that the buyer not sell the sold slave, because in it there is an increase devoid of consideration, so it becomes a cause of riba, or dispute will occur due to that condition. So the contract becomes devoid of its purpose, except if the condition is customary because custom prevails over analogy. And if the condition is such that the contract does not necessitate it and there is no benefit in it for anyone, then that condition does not invalidate the contract, and that is the apparent position of the school, like the condition that the buyer not sell the purchased animal, because the demand is nonexistent, so neither riba nor dispute occurs.

Elaboration: If someone sold his slave with the condition that the buyer free him, or make him mudabbar, or make him mukatab, or sold his slave-girl with the condition that the buyer make her umm walad, in all these cases the sale is fasid. The proof is that this is a sale with a condition, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited sale with condition. Therefore, the sale is fasid with these conditions.

The author of al-Hidayah presented a principle (dabita) regarding sale and condition, the summary of which is that there are several types of conditions:
(1) A condition that the contract necessitates, meaning from stipulating it the same benefit occurs that is established by the absolute contract, e.g., a slave sold with the condition that the buyer owns him – obviously even if this condition is not mentioned, the buyer still owns the slave. Or e.g., saying the condition of selling the slave is that the buyer hand over the price, or the buyer said I buy the slave on condition that the seller hand over the slave – in both cases, even without the condition, handing over the price is obligatory on the buyer and handing over the sold item is obligatory on the seller. In this type, the sale is permissible, and the condition is an extra thing, meaning the presence or absence of the condition is equal.
Question: If it is asked that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited sale and condition, and this hadith, due to its absoluteness, necessitates the impermissibility of the sale, so in this case the sale should be impermissible, whereas you have declared it permissible.
Answer: The answer is that this is not actually a condition because it provides the same thing that the absolute contract provides, so the condition's existence or non-existence is equal.
(2) A condition that the contract does not necessitate and in which there is benefit for the seller or buyer or subject matter, provided the subject matter is eligible for claiming, i.e., a human who can demand his right. For example, the seller sold a slave and stipulated that the buyer not sell this slave. In this condition, the benefit is for the subject matter (the slave) because the slave dislikes being a slave of different people and he can demand this. And if someone bought cloth with the condition that the seller sew it, then in this the buyer's benefit. And if someone sold a house with the condition that the seller reside in it for a month, then in this the seller's benefit. In these cases, the sale becomes fasid. The reason for invalidity is that when the seller and buyer exchanged the sold item for the price, the condition became devoid of consideration, and such an increase devoid of consideration is called riba, so due to this condition, riba becomes necessary, and riba is impermissible. And that which includes an impermissible matter is itself impermissible, so this sale is impermissible and fasid. The second reason for invalidity is that this condition causes dispute, so the contract becomes devoid of its purpose, which is to gain benefit without dispute, therefore this condition is invalidating. On the second reason for invalidity, al-Khadim has an objection: when the seller or buyer stipulates a condition and the other party accepts it, action will be according to the condition, so what dispute is there? It would have been better if the author had said that because the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited sale and condition, therefore in this case the sale is fasid.
(3) A condition in which one of the contracting parties has a benefit and it is contrary to the requirement of the contract, but it is customary, i.e., common practice. For example, someone bought shoes with the condition that the seller attach straps – this sale is not fasid, because that which is established by custom is as if established by a legal proof, because the Shari'ah's instruction is: 'What the Muslims consider good is good with Allah.' Or that a ruling established by custom is established by consensus, and consensus is a legal proof. Therefore, in case of conflict between custom and analogy, custom is given preference over analogy.
(4) A condition that the contract does not necessitate but there is no benefit for anyone in it. Like the seller stipulating that the buyer not sell the purchased animal. In this case, the apparent position of the school is that the sale is valid but the condition is void, meaning the condition is not heeded, because due to this condition the animal cannot demand (since it is not eligible to demand), so the meaning of riba does not apply, and no dispute occurs. Since these two things were the invalidators, when they are absent, the sale is not fasid. It is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf that in this case the sale becomes fasid.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Ashraf al-Hadaya on the Hidayah, addressing the ruling of conditions in sale contracts. It opens with the Arabic matn stating a principle: a condition that adds an extra benefit without consideration leads to riba or dispute, unless it is customary. The Urdu translation clarifies that if a slave or slave-girl is sold with conditions like emancipation, tadbir, kitabah, or making umm walad, the sale is fasid due to the hadith prohibiting sale with condition. The commentator then presents a framework (dabita) dividing conditions into four types:
1. Conditions that the contract inherently demands (e.g., stipulating ownership or payment) – these are valid and the sale is sound.
2. Conditions that the contract does not demand but provide a benefit to a party or the subject matter (if human) – these invalidate the sale due to riba or dispute.
3. Conditions that are contrary to the contract's requirement but are customary – these are valid because 'urf overrides qiyas.
4. Conditions with no benefit for anyone (e.g., restricting resale of an animal) – the sale is valid but the condition is void; Imam Abu Yusuf dissents.
The commentary explains the reasoning behind each type, addresses objections, and highlights the role of custom in overriding analogical reasoning. The discussion is essential for understanding when a condition in a sale is valid or invalid.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A stipulation made by one contracting party that adds an obligation or right beyond the contract's inherent effects.
ربا (riba): An increase devoid of consideration; here it refers to the extra benefit gained from the condition without corresponding compensation.
عرف ('urf): Customary practice that is prevalent among people; in this context, it overrides qiyas in validating conditions.
مقتضی عقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The inherent legal effects that the contract necessitates without explicit stipulation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following conditions would make a sale fasid?
A) Buyer stipulates that the seller delivers the item on a specific date.
B) Seller stipulates that the buyer will not resell the purchased slave.
C) Buyer stipulates that the seller sews a button on a shirt as a customary practice.
D) Seller stipulates that the buyer pays the price immediately.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a condition in a sale depends on its benefit, custom, and the contract's inherent requirements; not all conditions invalidate.

intermediate
8 min
Conditions That Invalidate the Sale (Beneficial Conditions)
Explains the first type of condition and its invalidating effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify conditions that benefit a party in a sale and explain why they render the sale fasid according to the Hanafi madhhab.

Core Idea
A condition that adds benefit without compensation introduces riba and potential dispute, voiding the sale.

In Hanafi fiqh, conditions not required by the contract are categorized by their effect. The most severe type is a condition that directly benefits one of the contracting parties or the subject of the sale (if the subject is a person). Such conditions make the sale fasid (voidable). This lesson unpacks the two primary reasons for this ruling: the introduction of riba-like extra without compensation, and the risk of future dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition type
Determine whether the condition benefits the buyer, the seller, or the subject of the sale (if the subject is a person capable of demanding rights). If yes, it falls into this category.
Benefit to a non-human subject does not count.
2
Explain the riba-based invalidity
The condition adds an extra right or service without any corresponding increase in the price. This extra is called riba in the sense of an unjustified increase, which is prohibited.
This is not the standard riba of loan but an analogous principle.
3
Explain the dispute-based invalidity
The condition may lead to future disagreement (e.g., about performance), which contradicts the contract's purpose of smooth benefit. However, the text notes an objection: if both parties consent, why dispute? The author prefers the hadith prohibition as the stronger basis.
This shows the internal refinement of reasoning.
4
Apply to new examples
Given a scenario (e.g., selling cloth with sewing condition), classify the condition as beneficial to one party, state that the sale is fasid, and give the two reasons.
Confirm that the sale is not batil but fasid; the contract is voidable.

شرط پر کہ بائع اس کو سی کر دے تو اس میں مشتری کا نفع ہے اور اگر کسی نے مکان بیچا اس شرط پر کہ بائع اس میں ایک ماہ قیام کرے گا تو اس میں بائع کا نفع ہے۔ اس صورت میں بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی۔ وجہ فساد یہ ہے آئے گا اور ر بوانا جائز ہے اور جو چیز نا جائز امر کو شامل ہو وہ خود نا جائز ہوتی ہے اس لئے یہ بیچ نا جائز اور فاسد ہوگی ۔ دوسری وجہ فساد یہ ہے کہ اس شرط کی وجہ سے جھگڑا پیدا ہوتا ہے تو عقد کا جو مقصود ہے یعنی بغیر جھگڑے کے نفع حاصل ہونا اس سے یہ عقد خالی ہو گا اس لئے یہ شرط مفسد ہے۔ دوسری وجہ فساد پر خادم کو ایک اشکال ہے وہ یہ کہ جب بائع یا مشتری نے کوئی شرط لگائی اور اس کے ساتھی نے اس کو منظور کر لیا تو اس شرط کے مطابق عمل ہوگا اس میں کیا جھگڑا ہے بہتر یہ تھا کہ فاضل مصنف یہ فرماتے کہ چونکہ رسول اللہ صلی علیہ وسلم نے بیع و شرطات منع فرمایا ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں بیع فاسد ہے۔ ۳) وہ شرط جس میں احد المتعاقدین کا نفع ہو اور وہ شرط مقتضی عقد کے خلاف ہو مگر وہ شرط متعارف ہو یعنی عرف عام کا معمول بہا ہو اور اس کا رواج ہو مثلاً کسی نے جوتا اس شرط کے ساتھ خریدا کہ بائع اس میں تسمہ بھی لگائے تو یہ بیچ فاسد نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ جو چیز عرف سے ثابت ہوتی ہے گویاوہ دلیل شرعی سے ثابت ہے۔ اس لئے کہ شارع علیہ الصلوۃ والسلام کا ارشاد ہے مار آہ المسلمون حسناً فهو عند الله حسن یعنی عمومی طور پر مسلمان جس کو پسند کر لیں وہ اللہ کے نزدیک بھی پسند ید یہ ہے ۔ یا یہ کہ جو حکم عرف سے ثابت ہے وہ اجماع سے ثابت ہے اور اجماع حجت شرعیہ ہے۔ اس لئے عرف اور قیاس کے درمیان تعارض کی صورت میں عرف کو قیاس پر ترجیح حاصل ہوگی۔ (۴) وہ شرط جس کا عقد تقاضہ نہیں کرتا لیکن اس میں کسی کا کوئی نفع بھی نہیں ہے۔ جیسے بائع کا یہ شرط لگانا کہ مشتری خریدے ہوئے جانور کو فروخت نہ کرے۔ اس صورت میں ظاہر مذہب یہ ہے کہ بیع صحیح ہو جائے گی لیکن شرط لغو ہوگی یعنی شرط کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس شرط کی وجہ سے مبیع یعنی جانور مطالبہ نہیں کرے گا اور مطالبہ اس لئے نہیں کرے گا کہ وہ استحقاق مطالبہ کا اہل نہیں ہے اور جب مطالبہ نہیں کرے گا تو ر ہوا کے معنی بھی لازم نہ آئیں گے۔ اور نہ کوئی جھگڑا واقع ہوگا حالانکہ یہی دو باتیں مفسد بیچ تھی پس جب دونوں باتیں نہیں پائی گئیں تو بیع فاسد بھی نہ ہوگی ۔ امام ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے کہ اس صورت میں بیچ فاسد ہو جائے گی ۔
The answer to this is that in reality this is not a condition, because this condition only conveys what the absolute contract already provides; thus the presence or absence of this condition is the same. The condition that the contract does not require, and in which there is benefit for the seller, the buyer, or the subject of the contract, provided that the subject is eligible to demand it, i.e., the subject is a human who can demand his right. For example, the seller sells a slave and stipulates that the buyer not sell this slave. In this condition there is benefit for the subject, i.e., the slave, because the slave dislikes being the slave of different people and can demand this. If someone buys cloth on condition that the seller sew it for him, then the buyer benefits. If someone sells a house on condition that the seller reside in it for a month, then the seller benefits. In this case the sale becomes fasid. The reason for fasid is that when the seller and buyer set the price in exchange for the goods, the condition becomes devoid of compensation, and such an addition devoid of compensation is called riba; thus this condition necessitates riba, and riba is not permissible, and whatever includes a prohibited thing becomes itself prohibited, so this sale is impermissible and fasid. The second reason for fasid is that this condition leads to dispute, so the intended purpose of the contract, which is obtaining benefit without dispute, is absent; therefore this condition is invalidating. There is an objection to the second reason: when a party stipulates a condition and the other accepts it, the condition will be acted upon, so where is the dispute? It would have been better if the author had said that because the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited sale with conditions, in this case the sale is fasid. 3) The condition in which one of the contracting parties benefits, and it is contrary to the requirement of the contract, but it is customary, i.e., commonly practiced. For example, someone buys shoes on condition that the seller also attach straps; this sale is not fasid. Because whatever is established by custom is as if established by a legal proof. The Prophet said: 'What the Muslims deem good is good before Allah.' Or it is said that a ruling established by custom is established by consensus, and consensus is a legal proof. Therefore, when custom and analogy conflict, custom is given precedence over analogy. 4) The condition that the contract does not require and in which there is no benefit for anyone. For example, the seller stipulates that the buyer not sell the purchased animal. In this case, the apparent madhhab is that the sale is valid, but the condition is void, because the animal cannot demand, since it is not eligible to demand its right. When it does not demand, the meaning of riba does not apply, and no dispute arises. Since these two things were the invalidators, and they are absent, the sale is not fasid. It is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf that in this case the sale becomes fasid.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah classifies conditions that are not required by the contract into three categories. First, conditions that benefit one of the parties or the subject (if the subject is a person capable of demanding his right). Examples include a condition that the buyer not resell a slave (benefit to the slave), that the seller sew the cloth (benefit to buyer), or that the seller stay in the house for a month (benefit to seller). Such conditions make the sale fasid (voidable) for two reasons: it introduces riba (because the condition is an extra without compensation) and it leads to potential dispute. The text notes an objection to the dispute reason: if both parties agree, there is no dispute. The author suggests the better justification is the hadith prohibiting conditions in sales. Second, a condition that benefits one party but is customary, like attaching straps to shoes; this is valid because custom is recognized as a legal proof, based on the hadith 'What Muslims deem good is good before Allah' and consensus. Third, a condition with no benefit at all, like forbidding the buyer to resell an animal; the sale is valid but the condition is void because the animal cannot demand its right. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, holding the sale fasid.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A stipulation in a sale contract that is not inherently required by the contract itself.
ربا (riba): Prohibited increase without compensation; here applied to extra condition without corresponding price adjustment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the primary reason a condition benefiting a party makes the sale fasid?
A) The condition creates uncertainty (gharar) in the contract.
B) The condition adds an extra benefit without compensation, analogous to riba.
C) The condition violates the buyer's ownership rights.
D) The condition is not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
A condition that grants extra benefit to a party or subject without offsetting the price renders the sale fasid due to the prohibition of riba and the potential for dispute.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Customary Conditions and Conditions Without Benefit
Discusses the second and third types of conditions and their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between customary conditions (valid) and conditions without benefit (valid sale, void condition) according to Hanafi law, including the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Customary conditions are valid and must be fulfilled; conditions benefiting no one result in a valid sale but a void condition.

Not all conditions in a sale contract cause invalidity. Hanafi fiqh recognizes two additional categories: (1) conditions that are customary (urf) — even if they benefit one party — are valid because custom has legal weight; and (2) conditions that provide no benefit to anyone result in a valid sale but the condition itself is void (لغو). This lesson explores the reasoning behind these rulings and notes the disagreement of Imam Abu Yusuf on the second category.

Guided Path
1
Identify customary conditions
A condition is customary if it is commonly practiced in the marketplace. Even if it benefits one party, it is valid because custom (urf) is a recognized legal proof.
Example: attaching straps to shoes when buying shoes.
2
Understand the legal basis for custom
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'What the Muslims deem good is good before Allah.' Also, consistent custom is deemed equivalent to consensus (ijma). Hence, custom takes precedence over analogy (qiyas).
This shows the importance of urf in Hanafi methodology.
3
Analyze conditions without benefit
If the condition stipulates something that does not benefit any party or the subject (e.g., forbidding sale of an animal), the sale is valid but the condition is void because there is no riba (no extra benefit) and no dispute risk.
The subject (animal) cannot demand, so no riba.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf holds such conditions also make the sale fasid. The apparent madhhab (zahir al-madhhab) is validity. The mufta bihi is the apparent madhhab.
This difference arises from how strictly one views the prohibition of conditions.

Source Text

The answer to this is that in reality this is not a condition, because this condition only conveys what the absolute contract already provides; thus the presence or absence of this condition is the same. The condition that the contract does not require, and in which there is benefit for the seller, the buyer, or the subject of the contract, provided that the subject is eligible to demand it, i.e., the subject is a human who can demand his right. There is an objection to the second reason: when a party stipulates a condition and the other accepts it, the condition will be acted upon, so where is the dispute? Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah classifies conditions that are not required by the contract into three categories. First, conditions that benefit one of the parties or the subject (if the subject is a person capable of demanding his right). Examples include a condition that the buyer not resell a slave (benefit to the slave), that the seller sew the cloth (benefit to buyer), or that the seller stay in the house for a month (benefit to seller). Such conditions make the sale fasid (voidable) for two reasons: it introduces riba (because the condition is an extra without compensation) and it leads to potential dispute. The text notes an objection to the dispute reason: if both parties agree, there is no dispute. The author suggests the better justification is the hadith prohibiting conditions in sales. Second, a condition that benefits one party but is customary, like attaching straps to shoes; this is valid because custom is recognized as a legal proof, based on the hadith 'What Muslims deem good is good before Allah' and consensus. Third, a condition with no benefit at all, like forbidding the buyer to resell an animal; the sale is valid but the condition is void because the animal cannot demand its right. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, holding the sale fasid.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Customary practice that is common and accepted by the people; given legal weight in determining the validity of conditions.
لغو (laghw): Void; a condition that has no legal effect and is not binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling on a condition that has no benefit (e.g., buyer not to sell a purchased animal)?
A) The sale is fasid because the condition is prohibited.
B) The sale is valid but the condition is void.
C) The sale is valid and the condition must be fulfilled.
D) The sale is batil.

Key Takeaway
Customary conditions are valid and enforceable; conditions without benefit result in a valid sale but the condition is void, though Imam Abu Yusuf differs.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on a Sale with Condition to Free a Slave
Intra-Hanafi dispute on a sale with condition to free

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a sale with a condition to free a slave, explain Abu Hanifa's reasoning based on suitability (mula'im), and determine the practical difference in rulings.

Core Idea
A sale with a condition to free is initially fasid, but Abu Hanifa holds it becomes valid upon actual freeing due to suitability in effect.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of a sale where the buyer stipulates that the slave must be freed. While the sale is considered fasid initially due to conflict with the contract's requirement of absolute disposal, the two sides differ on whether the actual act of freeing can cure the defect. Understanding this case sharpens your ability to distinguish between conditions that affect essence versus description, and to see how the concept of suitability (mula'im) operates in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed act and its initial ruling
The act is a sale of a slave with an explicit condition (shart) that the buyer must free the slave. All Hanafis agree that such a condition is not required by the contract, making the sale fasid (invalid in description) initially because it conflicts with the contract's requirement of free disposal.
Fasid here means valid in essence but invalid in description.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that upon the buyer actually freeing the slave, the condition's suitability (mula'im) in effect is realized because it perfects ownership. Therefore, the sale becomes valid (sahih) and the buyer owes the agreed price.
The key concept is that the condition is not suitable in itself but becomes suitable in its ruling (it leads to perfecting ownership).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) maintain that the sale remains fasid even after freeing. They argue that a fasid contract cannot become valid later, just as it cannot become valid if the slave perishes by another cause. Thus the buyer owes the market value (qimah), not the price.
Their basis is the general principle that a fasid contract is fixed as fasid.
4
Contrast the two reasoning paths
Abu Hanifa's reasoning relies on the idea that the condition's suitability is only actualized upon freeing, altering the ruling. The Sahibayn see no change because the initial fasid status is permanent. The dispute hinges on whether a fasid contract can be cured by subsequent events.
Both sides use valid Hanafi principles; the difference is in how they apply the concept of mula'im.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the buyer frees the slave, under Abu Hanifa the sale becomes valid and the buyer owes the agreed price. Under the Sahibayn, the sale remains fasid and the buyer owes only the market value (which may differ from the price). This affects the seller's entitlement.
The text does not specify a tarjih; later Hanafi practice often follows Abu Hanifa in matters of 'itq.

When this principle is established, we say that these conditions are not required by the contract, because the requirement of the contract is absolute disposal and choice, not compulsory obligation, while the condition requires that. And in it there is benefit for the subject. Al-Shafi'i, even if he opposes us in the condition of freeing and analogizes it to selling a slave as a gift (or by name?), the proof against him is what we mentioned. The interpretation of selling 'nasmah' is that he sells to someone whom he knows will free the slave, not that it is stipulated. So if the buyer, after purchasing with the condition of freeing, actually frees him, according to Abu Hanifa the sale becomes valid so that the price is due. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it remains fasid so that the value is due, because the sale was concluded as fasid and thus does not become valid, just as if it had perished in another way. Abu Hanifa's argument is that the condition of freeing, in terms of its essence, does not suit the contract as we mentioned, but in terms of its ruling it does suit it, because it leads to the perfection of ownership, and a thing is strengthened by its perfection. For this reason, freeing does not prevent return due to a defect. So if it perishes in another way, the suitability is not realized, so the fasid is established. But when freeing occurs, the suitability is realized, so the side of validity prevails, and the state before that was suspended. Translation: When this rule is established, we say that these conditions are not required by the contract, because the contract requires freedom in disposal and choice, not compulsory obligation, while the condition requires that, and in this condition there is benefit for the subject. Imam al-Shafi'i, although he opposes us in the condition of freeing and analogizes it to selling a slave by way of naming, the proof against him is what we mentioned. The interpretation of selling a slave by way of naming is that he is sold to a person whom it is known will free him, not that the condition of freeing is stipulated. Then if the buyer purchases the slave with the condition of freeing and frees him, according to Abu Hanifa the sale becomes valid, so that the price is due on the buyer. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said that the sale remains fasid, so that the value is due, because the sale occurred as fasid, so it does not become valid, just as if the slave had perished in another way. Abu Hanifa's argument is that the condition of freeing is suitable in terms of its essence, because it perfects ownership, and a thing becomes stable through its perfection. For this reason, freeing does not prevent returning due to a defect. So if it perishes in another way, the suitability is not realized, so the fasid is firmly established. But when freeing occurs, the suitability is realized, so the side of validity dominates, so the contract was suspended before that. Clarification: The author of Al-Hidayah says: When the rule regarding conditions is known, then observe that all the conditions mentioned in the matn are contrary to the requirement of the contract, because the contract wants the buyer to have full freedom of disposal and full choice to free or not, while the condition makes freeing obligatory. There is a conflict between the requirement of the contract and the requirement of the condition, and neither has precedence. So we act on both and say that this sale is fasid: it is valid in its essence (because the essential elements of a sale are present) but invalid in its description (because of the condition). Thus the sale is fasid. Imam al-Shafi'i agrees with us that a condition of tadbir, mukatabah, or making a slave an umm walad makes the sale fasid, but in the condition of freeing (al-‘itq) he differs.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage addresses the specific condition of freeing a slave (shart al-‘itq) in a sale. It begins by reiterating the principle that such conditions are not required by the contract and benefit the subject. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees with the Hanafi position on this condition, analogizing it to selling a slave to someone who is known to free him; but the Hanafi response distinguishes between an explicit condition and a mere expectation. The core of the passage is the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that if the buyer frees the slave after purchasing with the condition, the sale becomes valid (sahih) and the price is due. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) maintain that the sale remains fasid, so only the slave's value (qimah) is due, not the price. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the condition of freeing is suitable (mula'im) in its effect because it perfects ownership; thus upon the actual freeing, the suitability is realized and the validity side prevails. Before freeing, the contract is suspended (mawquf). The Sahibayn argue that a fasid sale cannot become valid later, just like any other fasid contract. The clarification from Sahib al-Hidayah explains that the conflict between the contract's requirement (free disposal) and the condition (compulsory freeing) results in the sale being fasid—valid in essence (because the elements of a sale exist) but invalid in description (because of the contradictory condition). Finally, it notes that Imam al-Shafi'i agrees with the Hanafis on conditions of tadbir, mukatabah, and umm walad but differs on the condition of freeing (al-‘itq).

Key Terms
شرط العتق (shart al-‘itq): A stipulation in a slave sale that the buyer must free the slave.
ملايم (mula'im): Suitable or harmonious; used to describe the condition that is compatible with the contract in its effect (perfecting ownership).
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a contract whose validity is pending upon the occurrence of a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the status of a sale with a condition to free a slave after the buyer actually frees him?
A) The sale remains fasid and the buyer owes the market value.
B) The sale becomes valid and the buyer owes the agreed price.
C) The sale is batil and must be repeated.
D) The condition is void but the sale is valid from the start.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on shart al-'itq illustrates how a fasid contract can be cured by fulfillment of a suitable condition, a concept unique to Abu Hanifa's reasoning.

Conditions Contradicting the Contract and the Shafi'i Dispute on Emancipation Condition
Inter-madhhab dispute on sale with emancipation condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules a sale fasid when a condition contradicts the contract's nature, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A condition that imposes a binding obligation contrary to the contract's default freedom renders the sale fasid.

This passage establishes a key principle of Hanafi contract law: the default sale contract grants the buyer unrestricted freedom of disposition and choice. A condition that imposes a binding obligation—like requiring the buyer to emancipate the slave—contradicts this nature and renders the sale fasid (defective). The author then addresses Imam al-Shafi'i's view that such a sale is valid, analogized to the practice of selling a slave to someone known to free him (nasmah). The Hanafi response clarifies that the practice involves no stipulation, so the analogy fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi principle
State the default rule: an absolute sale grants the buyer freedom of disposal and choice. A condition contrary to this nature invalidates the sale.
The condition imposes an obligation that the contract does not require; this is the core reasoning.
2
Locate the Shafi'i position and its analogy
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the sale valid, analogizing it to selling a slave to someone known to emancipate him (nasmah). Understand the analogy.
Nasmah means the buyer is known for freeing slaves, not that a condition is stipulated.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response distinguishes nasmah from a stipulated condition. In nasmah, there is no condition; the buyer is simply known to free slaves. The condition itself is what invalidates.
The hadith prohibiting a sale and a condition together supports the Hanafi position.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Shafi'i, the sale is valid and the price is owed. Under Hanafi, the sale is fasid; if the buyer consumes the slave, he owes the value (qima), and further ikhtilaf arises if he emancipates.
Fasid means the contract's essence is valid but a defect exists; rectification is possible under certain conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اذا ثبت هذا نقول هذه الشروط لا يقتضيها العقد لان قضية الاطلاق في التصرف والتخيير لا الا لزام حتما والشرط يقتضى ذالك وفيه منفعة للمعقود عليه والشافعي وان كان يخالفنا في العتق ويقيسه على بيع العبد نسمة فالحجة عليه ما ذكرناه وتفسير البيع نسمة ان يباع ممن يعلم انه يعتقه لا ان يشترط فيه
When this [principle] is established, we say: these conditions are not required by the contract, because the requirement of the contract [when left] absolute is freedom of disposal and choice, not a binding obligation, while the condition requires that [obligation], and in it there is benefit for the object of the contract. Al-Shafi'i, although he opposes us regarding emancipation and analogizes it to the sale of a slave by name (nasmah), the proof against him is what we have mentioned. The interpretation of 'sale of a slave by name' is that he is sold to someone who is known to emancipate him, not that a condition is stipulated for it.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage establishes a key principle in Hanafi contract law: the default contract (sale) grants the buyer unrestricted freedom of disposition and choice. A condition that imposes a binding obligation on the buyer (like emancipation) is contrary to the nature of the contract. Such conditions render the sale fasid (defective) because the contract's essence is valid but its description is invalid due to the condition. The passage then addresses Imam al-Shafi'i's view that selling a slave with the condition of emancipation is valid, analogizing it to the practice of selling a slave to someone known to emancipate him. The Hanafi response clarifies that there is a crucial difference: the known practice involves no stipulation; the condition itself is what invalidates. The proof includes the hadith prohibiting a sale combined with a condition, and rational reasoning that such conditions lead to disputes.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition stipulated in the contract that imposes a binding obligation on one party, contrary to the contract's default freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a sale conditioned on the buyer freeing the slave according to Abu Hanifa?
A) Valid and binding
B) Fasid (defective) but becomes valid if freed
C) Batil (void) from the start
D) Makruh but valid

Key Takeaway
A condition that imposes a binding obligation contradicting the contract's freedom renders the sale fasid; the Shafi'i analogy to nasmah is rejected because it lacks a stipulated condition.

Effect of Emancipation on Conditioned Sale: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute: validity of sale after emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether a conditioned sale becomes valid upon fulfillment of the condition and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A condition that initially invalidates a sale can later make it valid once its effect is realized.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding a sale of a slave conditioned on emancipation. The central question is whether the buyer's subsequent act of emancipation transforms a fasid contract into a valid one. Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ on the outcome, with each side providing a proof based on compatibility of the condition with the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Case and the Two Positions
A buyer purchases a slave with the condition that he will emancipate him. If the buyer actually frees him, what is the ruling? State Abu Hanifa's view (sale becomes valid, price due) and the Sahibayn's view (sale remains fasid, value due).
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's Proof
The Sahibayn argue that a fasid sale cannot become valid later. They analogize to the case where the slave dies for another reason: the fasd is established and not reversed. Identify the key principle: 'what is fasid does not become valid.'
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Proof (Talil)
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the essence of the condition (not compatible) and its ruling (compatible). The condition of emancipation leads to full ownership, which is compatible with the contract's purpose. Once emancipation occurs, compatibility is realized, and the contract becomes valid. The state before emancipation is suspended (mawquf).
4
Compare and Identify the Mufta Bihi
Contrast the two proofs. Note that the later Hanafi tradition, including many commentaries on Al-Hidayah, prefers Abu Hanifa's view as the mufta bihi. Understand the practical difference: price (thaman) vs. market value (qima).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو اعتقه المشترى بعد ما اشتراه بشرط العتق صح البيع حتى يجب عليه الثمن عند أبي حنيفة وقالا يبقى فاسدا حتى يجب عليه القيمة لان البيع قد وقع فاسدا فلا ينقلب جائز كما اذا تلف بوجه اخر وحجة ابي حنيفة ان شرط العتق من حيث ذاته لا يلائم العقد على ما ذكرناه ولكن من حيث حكمه يلائمه لانه منه للملك والشئى بانتها ئه يتقرر ولهذا لا يمنع العنق الرجوع بنقصان العيب فا ذاتلف من وجه آخر لم يتحقق الملايمة فيتقرر الفساد واذا وجد العتق تحققت الملايمة فترجح جانب الجواز فكان الحال قبل ذالك موقوفا
If the buyer frees him after purchasing him with the condition of emancipation, the sale is valid so that the price is obligatory upon him according to Abu Hanifa, and the two said: it remains fasid so that the value is obligatory upon him, because the sale occurred fasid and does not turn into valid, just as if it perished in another way. The proof of Abu Hanifa is that the condition of emancipation is not compatible with the contract in its essence, as we mentioned, but in its ruling it is compatible, because it leads to full ownership, and a thing is established by its full completion, and for this reason, emancipation does not prevent recourse for defect. So if it perishes in another way, compatibility is not realized, so the fasid is established. And when emancipation occurs, compatibility is realized, so the side of validity prevails, and the state before that is suspended.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the effect of the buyer actually freeing the slave after entering into a conditioned sale. According to Abu Hanifa, the sale becomes valid upon emancipation, and the buyer owes the agreed price. The Sahibayn hold that the sale remains fasid, and the buyer owes the market value (qima) of the slave. The proof for Abu Hanifa is that the condition of emancipation, while incompatible with the contract in its essence, becomes compatible in its effect because it leads to full ownership. Once emancipation occurs, the contract acquires compatibility and thus becomes valid. The Sahibayn argue that what was fasid cannot become valid by a subsequent act, similar to the case where the slave perishes for another reason. The Hanafi school's relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in some later works is the view of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): An irregular sale that is invalid due to a defect but can be corrected if the defect is removed.
شرط العتق (shart al-'itq): A condition inserted into the sale contract requiring the buyer to emancipate the slave.
ثمن (thaman): The agreed price in a sale contract.
قيمة (qima): The market value of the object, used when the contract is fasid and the object is consumed or transferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens to a sale of a slave conditioned on emancipation if the buyer actually frees the slave?
A) The sale remains fasid and the buyer pays value
B) The sale becomes valid and the buyer pays the agreed price
C) The sale becomes void and the slave is returned
D) The buyer must pay both price and value

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds that a conditioned sale becomes valid upon fulfillment of the condition if its effect is compatible with the contract's purpose, as in the case of emancipation leading to full ownership.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Selling a Slave with Condition of Emancipation: Inter- and Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Hanafi response to Shafi'i and internal ikhtilaf on bay' bi shart al-'itq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on bay' bi shart al-'itq, distinguish the proofs of the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools, and state the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of actual emancipation.

Core Idea
Sale with a condition contrary to muqtada al-'aqd is initially fasid, but if the condition is fulfilled, Abu Hanifa upholds validity while the Sahibayn do not.

This lesson explores a classical fiqh dispute: selling a slave with an explicit condition that the buyer must free him. The Hanafi school deems such a sale initially invalid (fasid), while the Shafi'i school considers it valid by analogy to selling to a virtuous person who is expected to free. Within the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples disagree on whether the sale becomes valid if the buyer actually emancipates. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of contract theory (muqtada al-'aqd) and the concept of fasid vs sahih in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the ruling of the Hanafi school on bay' bi shart al-'itq: the sale is initially fasid because the condition is against muqtada al-'aqd. Note the exception that occurs if the buyer frees the slave.
The initial ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the Opposing School and Their Proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the sale valid, based on qiyas to bay' al-'abd nassamah and the incident of Aisha buying Barirah.
Understand the basis of the Shafi'i qiyas.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response refutes the Shafi'i proofs: qiyas is invalid because it is qiyas al-shay' bi nafsihi; the Aisha incident involved a promise, not a condition; and bay' al-'abd nassamah means selling to a person known to free, not a conditional sale.
Focus on the logical refutation.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that if the buyer actually frees the slave, the sale becomes valid (sahih) because the condition becomes suitable to the contract. The Sahibayn hold it remains fasid, citing the principle that fasid cannot become valid (al-fasid la yanqalib ja'izan).
Note the difference in reasoning: suitability of condition vs. the principle of non-transformation.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the buyer pays the price (thaman); under the Sahibayn, the buyer pays the value (qimah). Under the Shafi'i school, the sale is valid and price is paid.
This affects financial liability.

باوجود نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا اختیار باقی رہنا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ مشتری کی ملکیت ختم نہیں ہوئی بلکہ اپنی انتہاء کو پہنچ کر مستحکم ہوگئی ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ اگر غلام موت یا قتل و غیرہ کسی وجہ سے ہلاک ہوا تو شرط عتق کا عقد کے مناسب ہونا متحقق نہیں ہوا بلکہ فساد اور زیادہ مضبوط ہو گیا اور جب عتق پایا گیا تو شرط کا عقد کے مناسب ہونا متحقق ہو گیا اور جواز کی جانب فساد کی جانب پر راج ہوگئی پس آزاد کرنے سے پہلے بیع کا حال موقوف رہے گا یعنی اگر بشرط اعتاق غلام بیچا تو بیع کا جواز اور فساد موقوف رہے گا چنانچہ غلام اگر اعتاق کے علاوہ کسی دوسری وجہ سے ہلاک ہو گیا تو بیع کا فاسد ہونا متحکم ہو جائے گا اور اگر مشتری نے اس کو آزاد کر دیا تو بیع تمام ہوکر جائز ہو جائے گی حالانکہ ابتداء میں فاسد تھی۔
They are against us. Thus, from them is a narration that selling with the condition of emancipation is permissible, and Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes this to bay' al-'abd nassamah. In al-Mabsut, bay' al-'abd nassamah is interpreted as sale with the condition of emancipation, meaning that a slave is sold on condition of freeing him. This means Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes sale with condition of emancipation to sale with a condition, and this analogy is from the category of qiyas al-shay' bi nafsihi, and qiyas al-shay' bi nafsihi is invalid, therefore this analogy is invalid. Question: If someone says that Aisha bought Barirah with the condition of emancipation and the Messenger of Allah declared it permissible, so it is proven that selling a slave with condition of emancipation is permissible. Answer: The answer is that Aisha bought Barirah absolutely without any condition, but she promised to emancipate her so that Barirah would consent, because Barirah was a mukatabah and the sale of a mukatabah without her consent is not permissible. Therefore Aisha first made Barirah consent by promising emancipation, then bought her from her master, and fulfilled her promise. It cannot be said that she bought her with the condition of emancipation. (Inayah) In response to Imam al-Shafi'i, the hadith we mentioned and rational proof are evidence against him, i.e., the Prophet prohibited sale and condition. Rational proof: the condition leads to dispute. And the interpretation of bay' al-'abd nassamah is that the slave is sold to a person from whose state it is known that he will free the slave. This never means that the slave is sold with a condition of emancipation. With this interpretation, there is no problem in selling the slave; we also hold its permissibility. The author of Hidayah said: Although sale with the condition of emancipation is impermissible, yet if someone sold a slave with the condition of emancipation and the buyer bought and freed him, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the sale becomes valid, so that the buyer owes the price, because in a valid sale the price is obligatory. According to the Sahibayn, it remains fasid as before, so that the buyer owes the value, because in a fasid sale the buyer owes the value. The Sahibayn's proof: this sale was initially fasid due to placing a condition contrary to the requirement of the contract, and the principle is that what is fasid cannot become valid, just as if the slave perished from another cause besides emancipation, e.g., he died or was killed after possession, in that case the sale remains fasid and the buyer owes the value. Similarly, if the buyer frees him, the sale remains fasid. And just as if the slave is sold with the condition of tadbir, mukatabat, or umm walad, and the buyer fulfills that condition, the sale remains fasid and does not become valid, and the buyer owes the value. Similarly, in the case of emancipation, the sale remains fasid and the buyer owes the value. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: The condition of emancipation is not suitable to this contract in its essence, as we mentioned earlier that the restriction of emancipation contradicts the buyer's free choice. But in terms of its ruling, it is suitable to this contract because emancipation perfects the buyer's ownership, and a thing upon reaching its perfection becomes strong and firm. If the buyer discovers a defect in the slave after emancipation, he has the right to recover the defect from the seller. Thus, the continuation of the right to recover defect after emancipation is proof that the buyer's ownership did not end but reached its perfection and became firm. The conclusion: If the slave perished from another cause like death or killing, the suitability of the condition to the contract is not realized; rather, the fasid is strengthened. When emancipation occurs, the suitability of the condition to the contract is realized, and the aspect of validity outweighs the aspect of fasid. So before emancipation, the state of the sale is suspended, i.e., if the slave is sold with the condition of emancipation, the validity and fasid of the sale are suspended. If the slave perishes from another cause, the sale becomes fasid. If the buyer frees him, the sale becomes complete and valid, even though it was initially fasid.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the ruling of selling a slave with the condition of emancipation (bay' bi shart al-'itq). The Hanafi position, as stated by the author of al-Hidayah, is that such a sale is initially invalid (fasid) because the condition is contrary to the requirement of the contract (muqtada al-'aqd). However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the effect if the buyer actually emancipates the slave after purchase. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the sale becomes valid because the condition, once fulfilled, becomes suitable to the contract, perfecting ownership. The Sahibayn maintain that the sale remains fasid because a contract that is initially invalid cannot become valid later (al-fasid la yanqalib ja'izan). The text also addresses the Shafi'i argument based on the incident of Aisha and Barirah, and refutes it by explaining that Aisha made a promise of emancipation, not a condition. The Hanafi proof includes the hadith prohibiting sale with an extra condition and rational arguments about dispute. The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated, but the detailed reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa is presented favorably.

Key Terms
بیع بشرط العتق (bay' bi shart al-'itq): Selling a slave with an explicit condition that the buyer must emancipate him.
بیع العبد نسمہ (bay' al-'abd nassamah): Selling a slave to a person who is known to be virtuous and likely to free him, without a condition in the contract.
مقتضی عقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The inherent legal effect and purpose of a contract; conditions contrary to this render the contract invalid.
فاسد (fasid): An invalid contract that requires dissolution; in sale, if already executed, buyer owes value (qimah) not price (thaman).
قیاس الشیء علی نفسہ (qiyas al-shay' bi nafsihi): An analogy that compares a thing to itself, which is logically invalid and rejected in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens to a sale of a slave with a condition of emancipation if the buyer actually frees the slave?
A) The sale remains fasid and the buyer owes the price.
B) The sale becomes valid and the buyer owes the price.
C) The sale is void and the slave is returned to the seller.
D) The sale becomes a gift and the buyer owes nothing.

Key Takeaway
A sale with a condition that contradicts the essence of the contract is initially invalid, but Imam Abu Hanifa allows it to become valid if the condition is fulfilled, emphasizing the purpose of the contract.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
6 min
Conditions of Service, Residence, Loan, or Gift in Sale
Ruling on invalid conditions that benefit one party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions that render a sale fasid in Hanafi fiqh and explain the dalil and reasoning behind the ruling.

Core Idea
A sale is fasid if it includes a condition that benefits one party and contradicts muqtada al-'aqd.

In this lesson, we examine four specific conditions that Hanafi jurists rule invalid when attached to a sale contract. These conditions provide a unilateral benefit to the seller and are contrary to the inherent purpose of the contract (muqtada al-'aqd). The passage explains the reasoning, citing prophetic prohibitions on combining sale with loan or two transactions in one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conditions and the ruling
List the four conditions: (1) seller takes service from sold slave, (2) seller resides in sold house, (3) buyer gives loan to seller, (4) buyer gives gift to seller. In all cases, the sale is fasid (invalid/voidable).
The sale is not batil but fasid; it can be rescinded or confirmed with a proper price.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade 'sale and loan' (bay' wa salaf) and 'two transactions in one' (safqataini fi safqa). These hadith serve as the main evidence.
The hadith is narrated by Ahmad and others; it is ahad but accepted in fiqh.
3
Understand the reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
If the service or residence has a portion of the price, it becomes a lease (ijarah) embedded in the sale. If not, it becomes a loan (ariyah) in the sale. Both are prohibited combinations of two contracts in one.
The condition of loan/gift directly falls under 'sale and loan' prohibition.
4
Apply the general principle
Any condition that is contrary to muqtada al-'aqd (the inherent purpose of the contract) and provides a unilateral benefit to one party corrupts the sale. This principle extends beyond the four examples.
Conditions that support the contract (e.g., delivery terms) are valid.

غلام اس شرط پر بیچا کہ بائع کی خدمت کرے گا یا گھر اس شرط پر بیچا کہ ایک مہینہ بائع اس میں ٹھہرے گا یا مشتری بائع کو قرض دے گا یا ہدیہ دے گا، ان شرائط کا حکم قال و کذالک لو باع عبدا على ان يستخدمه البائع شهرا او دارا على ان يسكنها او على ان يقرضه المشترى درهما او على ان يهدى له هدية لانه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وفيه منفعة لاحد المتعاقدين ولانه نهى عن بيع وسلف لانه لو كان الخدمة والسكنى يقابلهما شى من الثمن يكون اجارة فى بيع ولو كان لا يقابلهما يكون اعارة فى بيع وقد نهى النبي عن صفقتين في صفقة ترجمہ اور اسی طرح اگر غلام اس شرط پر بیچا کہ بائع اس سے ایک ماہ کی خدمت لے گا یا کوئی گھر اس شرط پر فروخت کیا کہ بائع اس میں سکونت کرے گا یا اس شرط پر کہ مشتری بائع کو ایک درہم قرضہ دے یا اس شرط پر کہ مشتری بائع کو کچھ ہدیہ دے کیونکہ یہ ایسی شرط ہے جس کا عقد تقاضہ نہیں کرتا ہے اور اس میں احد المتعاقدین کا نفع ہے اور اس لئے کہ آنحضرت صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے بیع اور قرض سے منع کیا ہے اور اس لئے کہ اگر غلام سے خدمت لینے اور گھر میں رہنے کے مقابل ثمن میں سے کوئی حصہ ہو تو بیع کے اندر اجارہ ہو جائے گا اور اگر خدمت اور سکونت دونوں کے مقابل ثمن کا کوئی حصہ نہ ہو تو بیع کے اندر عاریہ ہو جائے گا حالانکہ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے ایک صفقہ کے اندر دو صفقہ جمع کرنے سے منع کیا ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے غلام اس شرط پر فروخت کیا کہ وہ غلام ایک ماہ میری خدمت کرے گا یا مکان اس شرط پر فروخت کیا کہ بائع ایک ماہ اس میں سکونت کرے گا یا اس شرط پر فروخت کیا کہ بائع کو ایک درہم قرض دے یا اس شرط پر فروخت کیا کہ مشتری بائع کو کچھ ہدیہ دے ان تمام صورتوں میں بیع فاسد ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ تمام شرطیں مقتضی عقد کے خلاف ہیں اور ان شرطوں میں احد العاقدین یعنی بائع کا نفع بھی ہے۔ اور جو شرط مقتضی عقد کے خلاف ہو اور اس میں احد المتعاقدین کا نفع ہو وہ مفسد بیع ہوتی ہے اس لئے ان شرطوں کے ساتھ بیع فاسد ہوگی۔
If a slave is sold on condition that the seller will take service from him for a month, or a house is sold on condition that the seller will reside in it for a month, or on condition that the buyer will lend a dirham to the seller, or on condition that the buyer will give a gift to the seller. The ruling of these conditions: He said: And similarly, if he sells a slave on condition that the seller uses him for service for a month, or a house on condition that he resides in it, or on condition that the buyer lends him a dirham, or on condition that he gives him a gift, because it is a condition not required by the contract and it contains benefit for one of the contracting parties, and because he forbade sale and loan, and because if the service and residence have a portion of the price in exchange, then it becomes a lease within a sale, and if they have no portion in exchange, then it becomes a loan within a sale, and the Prophet forbade two transactions in one. Translation: And similarly, if a slave is sold on condition that the seller takes service from him for a month, or a house is sold on condition that the seller resides in it, or on condition that the buyer gives a loan of a dirham to the seller, or on condition that the buyer gives a gift to the seller, because this is a condition not required by the contract and it contains benefit for one of the contracting parties, and because the Prophet forbade sale and loan, and because if the service and residence have a portion of the price in exchange, then it becomes a lease in a sale, and if they have no portion in exchange, then it becomes a loan in a sale, although the Prophet forbade combining two transactions in one. Explanation: The scenario is that if someone sells a slave on condition that the slave will serve him for a month, or sells a house on condition that the seller will reside in it for a month, or sells on condition that the buyer gives a loan of a dirham to the seller, or on condition that the buyer gives a gift to the seller, in all these cases the sale is fasid. The proof is that all these conditions are contrary to the requirement of the contract, and these conditions contain benefit for one of the contracting parties, i.e., the seller. And any condition that is contrary to the requirement of the contract and contains benefit for one of the contracting parties is a corrupting factor of the sale. Therefore, with these conditions the sale is fasid.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding conditions in a sale that provide a benefit to one party and are contrary to the inherent purpose of the contract (muqtada al-'aqd). The author lists four conditions: (1) the seller takes service from the sold slave for a month, (2) the seller resides in the sold house for a month, (3) the buyer gives a loan to the seller, and (4) the buyer gives a gift to the seller. All these conditions make the sale invalid (fasid). The proof is from the hadith that the Prophet forbade 'sale and loan' (bay' wa salaf) and 'two transactions in one' (safqataini fi safqa). The reasoning is that if the service or residence has a portion of the price as consideration, it becomes a lease (ijarah) embedded in the sale, which is a combination of two contracts. If they have no consideration, it becomes a loan ('ariyah) in the sale, which is also prohibited. In Hanafi fiqh, any condition that is not in harmony with the contract and gives a unilateral benefit to a party invalidates the sale. The relied-upon position is that such sales are fasid, meaning they must be rescinded; if already executed, the buyer owes the market value of the object (qimah), not the agreed price.

Key Terms
مقتضی عقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The essential legal effects and obligations that a contract inherently requires; conditions contrary to this are invalid.
بیع وسلف (bay' wa salaf): A combined transaction of sale and loan; prohibited because it may involve riba or uncertainty.
صفقتین في صفقة (safqataini fi safqa): Two transactions combined into one; prohibited as it creates ambiguity and potential for injustice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is selling a house on condition that the seller continues to live in it for a month considered a fasid sale?
A) Because the condition makes the sale a gift.
B) Because it combines a sale with a lease or loan, which the Prophet prohibited.
C) Because the seller cannot benefit from the sold property.
D) Because the condition is against the rule of free choice.

Key Takeaway
A condition that provides unilateral benefit and contradicts the contract's purpose invalidates the sale in Hanafi fiqh.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling with Conditions Benefiting the Seller: Ruling and Proofs
Ruling and proofs for sale with conditions of service, residence, loan, or gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sales with conditions that solely benefit the seller and identify the three proofs from the hadith.

Core Idea
A condition that benefits the seller and is not required by the contract makes the sale fasid.

In Islamic commercial law, not every agreement term is valid. Some conditions, even if accepted by both parties, render the contract corrupt. The Hanafi school draws on hadith to prohibit sale conditions that only benefit the seller, such as retaining service, residence, or receiving a loan or gift. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenarios
List the four specific conditions mentioned: (1) seller uses slave for a month, (2) seller resides in sold house, (3) buyer lends money to seller, (4) buyer gives gift to seller.
These all benefit the seller solely.
2
State the Ruling
The sale in each case is fasid (void). Determine why: it is a condition not required by the contract (mudafi lil-aqd) and benefits one party.
Fasid means the contract is defective but can be rectified by removing the condition.
3
Trace the First Proof
The condition is not demanded by the nature of sale. Hanafi usul: any condition that contradicts the contract's requisites and benefits one party invalidates the contract.
This is a mabda' (principle) derived from the texts.
4
Examine the Hadith Proofs
Two hadith are cited: prohibition of sale+loan (bay' wa salaf) and prohibition of two transactions in one (safqatayn fi safqa). Explain how each applies to the scenarios.
The third proof is that such conditions turn the sale into a hire or loan within a sale, which is the duality prohibited.

دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ رسول اکرم صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے بیج اور قرض جمع کرنے سے منع فرمایا ہے یعنی ایسی بیج سے منع فرمایا ہے جس میں شرط مذکور ہو کہ مشتری بائع کو قرض بھی دے گا۔

تیسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام سے خدمت لینے اور گھر میں رہنے کے مقابلہ میں شمن کا کوئی حصہ ہوگا یا نہیں ؟ اگر ان دونوں کے مقابلہ میں شمن کا کوئی حصہ ہو تو یہ بیچ کے اندراجارہ ہوگا اور اگر ان کے مقابلہ میں ثمن نہ ہو تو یہ بیچ کے اندر عاریت پر لینا ہو گا یعنی بیچ کے اندراجارہ داخل ہوگا یا اعارہ داخل ہوگا حالانکہ مدینہ کے آقا رسول ہاشمی صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے ایک معاملہ کے اندر دو معاملے کرنے سے منع فرمایا ہے۔ یعنی ایک معاملہ کو دوسرے معاملہ کے اندر داخل کر کے دونوں کو جمع کرنے سے منع فرمایا ہے پس اس حدیث سے ثابت ہوا کہ خدمت لینے اور گھر میں رہنے کی شرط لگا نا شرط فاسد ہے اس کی وجہ سے بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
He said: Similarly, if he sold a slave with the condition that the seller use him for a month, or a house with the condition that he reside in it, or with the condition that the buyer lend him a dirham, or give him a gift, because it is a condition not required by the contract and contains benefit for one of the contracting parties, and because the Prophet forbade combining sale and loan, and because if the service and residence are compensated by part of the price, it becomes a hire within a sale, and if they are not compensated, it becomes a loan within a sale, and the Prophet forbade two transactions in one. Translation: And similarly, if a slave is sold with the condition that the seller will take service from him for a month, or a house is sold with the condition that the seller will reside in it, or with the condition that the buyer gives a loan of one dirham to the seller, or with the condition that the buyer gives the seller a gift, because this is a condition not required by the contract and contains benefit for one of the contracting parties, and because the Holy Prophet forbade sale and loan combined, and because if the service and residence are compensated by part of the price, it becomes a hire within a sale, and if they are not compensated, it becomes a loan within a sale, even though the Holy Prophet forbade combining two transactions in one contract. Explanation of the ruling: If someone sells a slave with the condition that the slave serve me for a month, or sells a house with the condition that the seller reside in it for a month, or sells with the condition that the seller gives a loan of one dirham, or sells with the condition that the buyer gives the seller a gift, in all these cases the sale is void. First proof: These conditions are contrary to the requirement of the contract and contain benefit for one party, the seller. A condition contrary to the contract that benefits one of the parties corrupts the sale. Second proof: The Holy Prophet forbade combining sale and loan, meaning a sale with a condition that the buyer will also give a loan. Third proof: The service and residence are either compensated by part of the price (making it a hire) or not (making it a loan). The Prophet forbade combining two transactions in one, so such conditions make the sale void.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on sales with conditions that solely benefit the seller, such as the seller retaining services, residence, receiving a loan, or a gift from the buyer. The ruling is that the sale is void (fasid). The author provides three proofs: first, the condition is not dictated by the nature of the sale and only benefits the seller, which corrupts the contract. Second, the hadith prohibits combining sale and loan (Bay' wa Salaf). Third, such a condition amounts to combining two transactions—either a hire or a loan within the sale—which is prohibited by the hadith 'two transactions in one'. The key takeaway is that any condition not required by the contract and conferring a benefit on one party renders the sale void in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): A condition that is invalid in the contract, rendering the contract itself corrupt (fasid).
بیع وسلف (bay' wa salaf): A sale combined with a loan, prohibited by hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a sale where the buyer agrees to lend the seller money as a condition of the sale?
A) The sale is valid and the loan is separate.
B) The sale is fasid (void).
C) The sale is valid if the loan is given later.
D) The sale is suspended pending the loan.

Key Takeaway
A sale becomes fasid when it includes a condition that benefits the seller and is not required by the contract, as proven by the hadith prohibiting bay' wa salaf and two transactions in one.

Selling a Specific Asset with Deferred Delivery
Ruling with rational proof on a term in sale of 'ayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why a condition of deferred delivery in the sale of a specific existing asset renders the sale void (fasid) in the Hanafi school and distinguish this from a valid salam contract.

Core Idea
A term for delivery is only valid for debts, not for specific existing goods.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle of Hanafi sales law: a sale of a specific existing asset (ʿayn) with a condition that delivery be deferred to a future date is void (fasid). The Hidayah explains that a term (ajal) is legislated for ease and is therefore suitable for debts, which require time to procure, not for assets already in hand. Understanding this distinction clarifies why the Hanafi school treats such sales differently from salam contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale of a specific asset (ʿayn) with a condition that delivery is deferred to a future date (e.g., beginning of the month). The ruling in the Hanafi school is that the sale is fasid (void).
Fasid means it is invalid and cannot be corrected; the contract must be redone properly.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is rational (dalil ʿaqli): a term is prescribed for ease (tarfih). It allows time to procure the object of sale, which is needed for debts (e.g., dirhams/dinars) but unnecessary for an already existing asset.
No direct Quran or hadith is cited; the reasoning relies on the purpose of the term.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Since the term exists only for ease in acquisition, and the specific asset already exists, a delivery term adds no utility. Stipulating it is a void condition (shart fasid), which corrupts the sale.
The condition is not just makruh; it renders the contract legally defective.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with the Shafiʿi school
The Shafiʿi school generally permits a term in the sale of a specific asset if both parties agree, as long as it does not contradict the contract. The Hanafi school restricts terms to debts only.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; each school has its own usul basis.
5
Distinguish from salam (forward sale)
In salam, the subject matter is a debt (dayn) in the seller's liability, not a specific existing asset. A term is suitable because the goods are fungible and need time to be procured. Thus, salam is valid despite deferred delivery.
The Hidayah author adds the word 'fī' (in) after 'sale' to exclude salam's subject matter.

ایک چیز اس شرط پر بیچی کہ مہینے کے آغاز تک سپر دنہیں کرے گا، بیع کا حکم
قال ومن باع عينا على ان لا يسلمه الى رأس الشهر فالبيع فاسد لان الاجل في البيع العين باطل فيكون شرطا فاسدا وهذا لان الاجل شرع ترفيها فيليق بالديون دون الاعيان
ترجمہ کہا اور اگر کسی نے عین شئی اس شرط پر فروخت کی کہ وہ اس کو چاند رات تک سپر نہیں کرے گا تو بیع فاسد ہے کیونکہ مبیع عین میں میعاد باطل ہے لہذا یہ شرط فاسد ہوگی کیونکہ میعاد آسانی کے لئے مشروع ہوتی ہے پس یہ دیون کے لائق ہے نہ کہ اعیان کے۔ تشریح مسئلہ اگر کسی نے مال عین یعنی در اہم ود نائیر کے علاوہ کسی متعینہ چیز کو اس شرط کے ساتھ فروخت کیا کہ مبیع پہلی تاریخ کو یا آخری تاریخ کو سپر دکروں گا تو یہ بیع فاسد ہوگی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مہلت اور میعاد آسانی کے لئے مشروع ہوئی ہے یعنی میعاد اس لئے مشروع کی گئی ہے تا کہ اس مدت میں اس شئی کا حاصل کرنا آسان ہو جائے جس کے بارے میں میعاد مقرر کی گئی پس میعاد دیون یعنی در اہم ودنا نیر کے مناسب ہے اعیان کے مناسب نہیں ہے کیونکہ مال عین تو بالفعل موجود ہوتا ہے اور دراہم ودنا نیر آہستہ آہستہ تلاش کئے جاتے ہیں پس جو چیز بالفعل موجود ہے اس میں میعاد کی چنداں ضرورت نہیں ورنہ خواہ مخواہ تحصیل حاصل کا مرتکب ہونا پڑے گا ہاں جو چیز بالفعل موجود نہیں ہے اس میں میعاد اور مہلت دینا مفید اور کارآمد ہے۔ پس جب میعاد اعیان کے مناسب نہیں ہے تو سبیع عین یعنی معینہ موجودہ بیع میں اجل اور میعاد کا ذکر کرنا باطل ہوگا اور میعاد کی شرط لگانا شرط فاسد ہوگا اور شرط فاسد سے چونکہ بیع فاسد ہو جاتی ہے اس لئے شرط کے ساتھ بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے بیچ کے بعد لفظ مین بڑھا کر مسلم فیہ کو خارج کر دیا ہے کیونکہ بیع سلم میں مسلم فیہ صبیح تو ہوتی ہے مگر عین نہیں ہوتی بلکہ مسلم الیہ کے ذمہ میں واجب ہوتی ہے اور اس کی سپردگی ایک مدت کے بعد ہوتی ہے اس لئے مسلم فیہ کے لئے اجل اور میعاد ناسب ہے۔
He said: Whoever sells a specific item (ayn) with the condition that he will not deliver it until the beginning of the month, the sale is void because a term in the sale of a specific item is invalid, so it is a void condition. This is because a term is legislated for ease, so it is suitable for debts, not for specific items. Translation: He said: And if someone sells a specific thing with the condition that he will not deliver it until the night of the moon (end of month), the sale is void because a term (ajal) in the sale of a specific good is invalid, hence this is a void condition. Because a term is prescribed for ease, so it is suitable for debts, not for specific goods. Explanation: If someone sells a specific property, i.e., something other than dirhams and dinars, with the condition that I will deliver the item on the first date or the last date, then the sale is void. The proof is that a term is legislated for ease, meaning it is to make it easy to obtain the thing in that period. So a term is suitable for debts like dirhams and dinars, not for specific goods, because a specific good exists already, while dirhams and dinars are gradually sought. So for something already existing, a term is not needed, otherwise it leads to unnecessary effort. For something not existing, a term is useful. Therefore, mentioning a term in the sale of a specific existing good is invalid, and the condition of a term is a void condition, and a void condition makes the sale void. The author of Hidayah added the word 'mayn' (in) after 'sale' to exclude the subject matter of salam, because in salam, the subject matter is a thing (shay') but not a specific good; rather it is an obligation in the liability of the seller, and its delivery is after a term, so a term is suitable for the subject matter of salam.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on selling a specific asset (ayn) with a condition delaying delivery to a future date. The ruling is that such a sale is void (fasid) because a term (ajal) is legislated for ease (tarfih) and is appropriate for debts (dayn), not for specific existing assets. The reasoning is that a term serves to allow time to procure the object, which is needed for debts like money but unnecessary for goods already in hand. Specifying a delivery date for an existing item would be redundant. This ruling is distinguished from salam (forward sale), where the subject matter is a debt in the seller's liability, making a term suitable. Thus, the condition of deferred delivery in the sale of a specific item corrupts the sale.

Key Terms
عین ('ayn): A specific, existing tangible asset (e.g., a slave, a house, a specific piece of gold).
اجل (ajal): A fixed term or deferment for delivery or payment.
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A defective sale that is not fundamentally void but includes an invalid element; it can be corrected by removing the defect before the transaction is finalized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a sale of a specific item with a term for delivery considered void in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the term is not specified clearly.
B) Because a term is only suitable for debts, not for existing assets.
C) Because the buyer might not pay on time.
D) Because the seller cannot guarantee delivery.

Key Takeaway
Stipulating a delivery term in the sale of a specific existing asset renders the sale fasid because a term is only appropriate for debts, not for ʿayn.
